Critiquing the Author's Division of Knowledge: Method as the Criterion
Critique of the author's division of knowledge based on combination with judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the author's division of knowledge and reconstruct the correct division based on method.

Core Idea
The method of access, not content, should determine the division of knowledge.

The proper division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq is not arbitrary; it must be based on the unique method by which each type is obtained. The author of our text proposed a division based on whether perception is combined with judgment, but this leads to absurd consequences. In this lesson, we dissect the critique and see why method is the correct criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's division
The author divided knowledge into perception not combined with judgment and perception combined with judgment, making the latter tasdiq.
2
Understand the objector's premise
The objector (the commentator) notes that even pure perception of a subject involves some implicit relation, making it 'combined with judgment' in a sense.
3
See the absurd consequence
If perception of subject alone is combined with judgment, then it would count as tasdiq, proliferating tasdiqs and collapsing the distinction.
4
Recognize the correct criterion
The correct division uses method: tasdiq arises from argument (hujja), tasawwur from expository statement (qawl sharih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنما ينفرد بطريق خاص يوصل إليه، وهو الحجة المنقسمة إلى أقسامها، وماعدا هذا الإدراك له طريق واحد يوصل إليه، وهو القول الشارح، فتصور المحكوم عليه وتصور المحكوم به وتصور النسبة الحكميّة يشارك سائر التصورات في الاستحصال بالقول الشارح، فلافائدة في ضمها إلى الحكم وجعل المجموع قسماً واحداً من العلم المسمّى بالتصديق، لأن هذا المجموع ليس له طريق خاص . فمن لاحظ مقصود الفن - أعني بيان الطريق الموصل إلى العلم - لم يلتبس عليه أن الواجب في تقسيمه ملاحظة الامتياز في الطرق، فيكون الحكم أحد قسميه المسمّى بالتصديق، لكنه مشروط في وجوده وتحققه إلى ضم أمور متعددة من أفراد القسم الآخر. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا أردت تقسيم العلم على هذا المذهب قلت : العلم - أي الإدراك، مطلقاً - إما أن يكون إدراكاً لأن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة، وإما أن يكون إدراكاً لغير ذلك ؛ فالأول يسمى تصديقاً والثاني تصوراً. وإذا أردت تقسيمه على مذهب الإمام قلت : العلم إما أن يكون إدراكاً الأمور أربعة - هي المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به والنسبة الحكمية وكون تلك النسبة واقعة أو غير واقعة - و إما أن يكون إدراكاً هو غير ذلك الإدراك المذكور ؛ فالأول هو التصديق، والثاني هو التصوّر.
وأما تقسيم المصنف، فلايصح على مذهب الحكماء قطعاً - لأن التصديق عندهم هو الحكم وحده، لا التصوّر الذي معه الحكم - ولا على مذهب الإمام أيضاً.
وبيان ذلك أن حاصل ماذكره المصنف أن أحد قسمي العلم - هو إدراك غير مجامع للحكم، والقسم الثاني هو إدراك مجامع للحكم. ويرد عليه أن تصوّر المحكوم عليه وحده إدراك مجامع للحكم، فيلزم أن يخرج عن القسم الأوّل ويدخل في الثاني، فيكون تصوّر المحكوم عليه وحده و
كذا يكون تصور المحكوم به وحده تصديقاً آخر، ويكون تصوّر النسبة المقارن
For it is uniquely reached by a specific method, namely the argument (hujja) divided into its divisions. And apart from this perception, there is one method to reach it, namely the expository statement (qawl sharih). Thus the perception of the subject, the perception of the predicate, and the perception of the predicative relation share with the rest of the concepts in being obtained by the expository statement. So there is no benefit in joining them to the judgment and making the sum a single division of knowledge called tasdiq, because this sum does not have a specific method. So whoever considers the purpose of the art — i.e., the explanation of the method leading to knowledge — will not be confused that the obligation in its division is to consider the distinction in methods. Thus the judgment is one of its two divisions called tasdiq, but it is conditioned in its existence and realization on the inclusion of multiple things from individuals of the other division. And if you know this, we say: If you want to divide knowledge according to this school, you say: knowledge — i.e., perception absolutely — is either a perception that a relation is actual or not actual, or a perception of something else; the first is called tasdiq and the second tasawwur. And if you want to divide it according to the view of the Imam, you say: knowledge is either a perception of four things — the subject, the predicate, the predicative relation, and that relation being actual or not — or a perception other than that mentioned perception; the first is tasdiq, the second tasawwur. As for the author's division, it is not valid on the view of the philosophers at all — because tasdiq for them is only the judgment, not the concept that accompanies the judgment — nor on the view of the Imam either. The explanation of that is that the gist of what the author mentioned is that one of the two divisions of knowledge is a perception that is not combined with the judgment, and the second division is a perception combined with the judgment. It is objected that the perception of the subject alone is a perception combined with the judgment, so it would necessarily leave the first division and enter the second, so the perception of the subject alone would be another tasdiq, and likewise the perception of the predicate alone would be another tasdiq, and the perception of the relation combined...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage argues for the correct division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) based on the method (tariq) by which each is obtained. The author begins by stating that the judgment (hukm) is uniquely obtained by argument (hujja), whereas all other perceptions — including the perception of subject, predicate, and relation — are obtained by the expository statement (qawl sharih). Therefore, it is futile to group these perceptions with the judgment under tasdiq, because that sum lacks a unique method. The correct division must consider the distinct methods: on the philosophers' view, tasdiq is the perception that a relation is actual or not (judgment), and tasawwur is all else. On the view of Imam Fakhr al-Razi, tasdiq is the perception of four things (subject, predicate, relation, and actuality), while tasawwur is any other perception. Then the author critiques the division given by the author of the original text (al-musannif). The author's division makes one part a perception not combined with judgment and the other part a perception combined with judgment. The objection is that the perception of the subject alone is already combined with judgment (because one cannot conceive a subject without implicitly relating it to something), so it would fall into the second division, making it a tasdiq, which is absurd. The critique is cut off on this page. The passage uses technical terms from Islamic logic and epistemology, emphasizing the purpose of the discipline (mantiq) as explaining the method to knowledge. No secular examples are used; instead, it relies on conceptual analysis within the tradition.

Key Terms
الطريق
القول الشارح
تقسيم المصنف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the author's division of knowledge?
A) Because the author used a wrong method
B) Because it leads to the absurdity that simple conception becomes tasdiq
C) Because the author did not cite the philosophers
D) Because the division has too many parts

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge must be based on the method of acquisition, not on whether it involves judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a passage in the commentary of al-Qutb al-Tahtani where he divides knowledge. Apply the method-based criterion to check if his division is valid.
?
Construct the correct division of knowledge according to the philosophers and according to Imam Fakhr al-Razi as stated in the passage.

The Four Causes in the Definition of Thought
Author explains how the definition of thought includes the four causes with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the definition of thought (fikr) implicitly contains all four causes and distinguish between direct and metaphorical applications of causal terms.

Core Idea
The definition of thought is complete because it contains all four causes analogically.

In this passage, the author unpacks the definition of fikr (thought) as 'ordering known matters to attain an unknown.' He shows that this simple definition, upon analysis, contains all four Aristotelian causes—formal, efficient, material, and final—making it the most complete kind of definition. However, he also cautions that some of these causal terms are applied metaphorically to the psychic act of thinking.

Guided Path
1
Map the definition to the four causes
The author links 'ordering' (tartib) to the formal and efficient causes, 'known matters' (umur ma'luma) to the material cause, and 'attaining an unknown' (ta'diya ila majhul) to the final cause. Write down each part of the definition and the corresponding cause.
2
Distinguish direct and implied indication
Note that 'ordering' directly indicates the formal cause (mutabaqa) and implies the efficient cause (iltizam). What does this distinction mean for how the causes are present in the definition?
3
Evaluate the analogical extension
The author states that matter and form properly belong to bodies, but they are applied to thought metaphorically. Why does he allow this analogical usage, and what does it tell us about the nature of definitions in logic?
4
Assess completeness
The author says a definition with all four causes is more complete. Consider why this is the case. Can you think of a definition from another Islamic science that might be strengthened by including all four causes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن لطائف هذا التعريف أنه مشتمل على العلل الأربع فـ«الترتيب» إشارة إلى العلة الصورية بالمطابقة، فإن صورة الفكر هي الهيأة الاجتماعية الحاصلة للتصورات والتصديقات، كالهيأة الحاصلة لأجزاء السرير في اجتماعها وترتيبها. وإلى العلة الفاعلية بالالتزام، إذ لا بد لكلِّ ترتيب من ،مرتب، وهي القوة الفاعلة، كالنجار للسرير. (۱) كل مركب صادر عن فاعل مختار لابد له من علّة مادية وعلة صورية - وهما داخلتان فيه - ومن علّة فاعليّة وعلة غائيّة، وهما خارجتان عنه؛ وقديعرف الشيء بالقياس إلى علة واحدة أو علتين أو ثلاث، وإذا عرف بالأربع كان ذلك أكمل من باقي الأقسام ؛ وليس المراد من التعريف بالعلل أن تكون هي بنفسها معرفة - لأنها مباينة للمعلول - بل المراد أنه يؤخذ للمعلول بالقياس إلى العلل محمولات عليه فيعرف بها. وماذكره من أن «فاعل النظر هو المرتب الناظر، وأن غايته هو التأدي إلى مجهول» فهو قول تحقيقي، وأمّا أنّ الأمور المعلومة مادية، وأن الهيأة العارضة لتلك الأمور صورية فهو قول على سبيل التشبيه، لأن النظر من الأعراض النفسانية والمادة والصورة إنما تكونان للأجسام (شريف). و «أمور معلومة» إشارة إلى العلة المادية، كقطع الخشب للسرير. و« للتأدي إلى مجهول» إشارة إلى العلة الغائية، فإن الغرض من ذلك الترتيب ليس إلا أن يتأدّى الذهن إلى المطلوب المجهول، كجلوس السلطان - مثلاً - للسرير.
Among the subtleties of this definition is that it includes the four causes. 'Ordering' indicates the formal cause directly (mutabaqa), for the form of thought is the composite structure that results for concepts and assents, like the structure that results for the parts of a bed in their assembly and ordering. And it indicates the efficient cause by implication (iltizam), because every ordering must have an orderer, which is the active faculty, like the carpenter for the bed. Every composite proceeding from a free agent necessarily has a material cause and a formal cause—both internal to it—and an efficient cause and a final cause—both external to it. A thing may be known in relation to one, two, or three causes, and if it is known by all four, that is more complete than the other types. The intent of definition by causes is not that the causes themselves are known—because they are distinct from the effect—but rather that the effect is taken in relation to the causes as predicates predicated on it, and thus it is known by them. What he mentioned—that 'the agent of thought is the orderer who thinks, and its goal is the attainment of an unknown'—is a definitive statement. As for saying that the known matters are material and that the accidental structure of those matters is formal, that is a metaphorical statement, because thought is among the psychic accidents, while matter and form only belong to bodies. (Sharif) 'Known matters' indicates the material cause, like the pieces of wood for the bed. And 'attainment of an unknown' indicates the final cause, for the purpose of that ordering is only that the mind attains the desired unknown, like the king's sitting—for example—on the bed.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines thought (fikr) by showing how the definition 'ordering of known matters leading to an unknown' implicitly contains the four causes. The author first notes the formal cause: 'ordering' (tartib) directly indicates the formal cause, because the form of thought is the composite structure of concepts and assents, analogous to the structure of a bed. Then 'ordering' implicitly indicates the efficient cause, because every ordering requires an orderer, i.e., the thinking faculty. Next, 'known matters' indicates the material cause, like the wood pieces of a bed. Finally, 'leading to an unknown' indicates the final cause, the purpose being to reach the desired unknown, like the king's sitting on a bed. The author notes that knowing something by all four causes is the most complete form of definition. However, the material and formal causes are internal to the thing, while the efficient and final are external. Also, the statement that the known matters are material and the form is accidental is analogical, since thought is a psychic accident, while matter and form properly belong to bodies.

Key Terms
العلة الصورية (al-illa al-surawiyya): The formal cause of thought is the composite structure (hay'a ijtima'iyya) resulting from the arrangement of concepts and assents.
العلة الفاعلية (al-illa al-fa'iliyya): The efficient cause is the thinking faculty (al-quwwa al-fa'ila) that performs the ordering.
العلة المادية (al-illa al-maddiyya): The material cause is the known matters (al-umur al-ma'luma) that constitute the content of thought.
العلة الغائية (al-illa al-gha'iyya): The final cause is the attainment of the unknown (al-tahdi ila majhul) which is the purpose of thought.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the term 'ordering' (tartib) indicate the efficient cause?
A) By direct indication (mutabaqa)
B) By implicit implication (iltizam)
C) By metaphorical extension
D) It does not indicate the efficient cause

Key Takeaway
The definition of thought is complete because it contains all four causes, but the application of material and formal causes to thought is analogical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the definition of 'wudu' (ablution) in fiqh: 'washing specific limbs with water with intention.' Identify which parts of this definition correspond to the four causes: material, formal, efficient, and final. Explain whether any cause is implied or directly stated.
?
Task 2: The commentator says that applying 'material cause' to thought is metaphorical. Find another example in Islamic sciences where a causal term (e.g., 'illa) is used analogically rather than literally. Explain why the analogy holds.

Why a Science's Introduction Must Be a Rasm
Author explains why definition of a science for beginners must be a rasm, not a hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why the initial definition of a science for a beginner must be a rasm (description) rather than a hadd (essential definition), using the argument that the hadd requires prior knowledge of all the science's issues.

Core Idea
A science's hadd needs complete knowledge of its issues, so beginners must start with its rasm.

When you open a textbook on a new Islamic science, you are first given a definition. This lesson reveals why that definition cannot be a complete essential definition (hadd) but must be a descriptive sketch (rasm). The author shows that the hadd is only attainable after mastering the entire science, so for the beginner, a rasm is the necessary starting point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Find the sentence that states that the hadd of a science requires knowing all its issues, and therefore the introductory definition must be a rasm.
Look for 'فمعرفته بحسب حده وحقيقته لا تحصل إلا بالعلم بجميع مسائله'.
2
Extract the reason why hadd is impossible at the start
Why is it impossible to give a hadd at the beginning? Because 'not that is a preface for starting into it' – you cannot know the essence before studying the issues that constitute it.
3
Observe the author's explicit linguistic choice
The author emphasises that the matn author used 'rasamooh' (they described it) instead of 'haddooh' (they defined it). This is a deliberate signal to the student that this is a rasm, not a hadd.
The phrase 'تنبيهاً على أن مقدمة الشروع في كل علم رسمه' is key.
4
Apply to a real Islamic science
Think of how 'ilm al-usul is introduced: it is described as 'knowledge of the evidences of fiqh in general' – that is a rasm, not a hadd capturing its essence. Can you imagine what a hadd would require? You would need to know all its issues like types of evidences, methods of deduction, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماهية وحقيقة وراء تلك المسائل، فمعرفته بحسب حده وحقيقته لا تحصل إلا بالعلم بجميع مسائله، وليس ذلك مقدمة للشروع فيه، إنما المقدمة معرفته بحسب رسمه فلهذا صرّح بقوله : «ورسموه»، دون أن يقول : وحَدُّوه» - إلى غير ذلك من العبارات - تنبيهاً على أن مقدمة الشروع في كل علم رسمه، لاحده. فإن قلت : العلم بالمسائل هو التصديق بها ومعرفة العلم بحده تصور، والتصور لا يستفاد من التصديق. قلت : العلم بالمسائل هو التصديق بالمسائل حتى إذا حصل التصديق بجميع المسائل حصل العلم المطلوب، لكن تصوّر العلم المطلوب محله يتوقف على تصوّر تلك التصديقات - لا على نفسها - فالتصور غير مستفاد من التصديق.
Essence and reality are beyond those issues, so knowledge of it according to its definition (hadd) and reality is not obtained except by knowing all its issues, and that is not a preface for starting into it. Rather, the preface is knowing it according to its description (rasm). For this reason he explicitly said 'and they described it', instead of saying 'and they defined it (hadd)' – among other expressions – as a notification that the preface for starting any science is its description (rasm), not its definition (hadd). For if you say: Knowledge of the issues is assent (tasdiq) to them, and knowledge of the science by its definition is a concept (tasawwur), and a concept is not derived from assent. I say: Knowledge of the issues is assent to the issues, so if assent to all issues is obtained, the desired science is obtained. But the conceptualization of the desired science – i.e., its definition – depends on the conceptualization of those assents – not on the assents themselves – so the concept is not derived from the assent.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage continues the explanation of why the definition of a science given at the beginning is a rasm, not a hadd. The author argues that to know the essence (hadd) of a science requires knowing all its issues, which is impossible before studying it. Therefore, the introductory definition must be a rasm, i.e., based on external or descriptive features. He notes that the author of the matn deliberately used 'rasamooh' (they described it) instead of 'haddooh' (they defined it) to emphasize this point. Then an objection is raised: knowledge of the issues is assent (tasdiq), while knowledge of the definition is a concept (tasawwur). If the definition depends on knowing all issues, then the concept would be derived from assent, which is impossible because tasawwur is prior to tasdiq. The author's answer distinguishes: the concept of the science itself is not derived from the assents themselves, but from the conceptualization (tasawwur) of those assents. In other words, knowing the issues as a mental object provides the material for the concept of the science, not the assents directly. This preserves the epistemological order: tasawwur precedes tasdiq.

Key Terms
حد
رسم
مسائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why did the author of the matn use 'rasamooh' instead of 'haddooh'?
A) Because a rasm is more accurate
B) Because the definition of a science for beginners must be a rasm, not a hadd
C) Because hadd is not permissible in logic
D) Because rasm is easier

Key Takeaway
A science's introductory definition is necessarily a rasm, because the hadd requires complete knowledge of all the science's issues, which the beginner lacks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Resolving the Objection: How Tasawwur Arises from Tasdiq
Author addresses objection about tasawwur/tasdeeq in defining a science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the conceptualization (tasawwur) of a science can be derived from the conceptualization of its assented issues (tasdiq), thus preserving the epistemological priority of tasawwur over tasdiq.

Core Idea
The concept of a science comes from conceptualizing its assented issues, not from the assents themselves.

A careful reader might object: if the definition of a science is taken from its issues, and issues involve assent (tasdiq), then the concept (tasawwur) would come after assent, violating the principle that tasawwur precedes tasdiq. The author resolves this by distinguishing between the assents themselves and our conceptualization of those assents. This lesson unpacks that clever distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection says: knowledge of issues is tasdiq (assent), and the knowledge of the science by its definition is tasawwur (concept). But a concept is not derived from assent (because tasawwur is prior). So how can the definition come from issues?
The objection is phrased as 'فإن قلت'.
2
Find the distinction in the response
The author answers by separating 'the assents themselves' from 'the conceptualization of those assents'. The concept of the science depends on the conceptualization (tasawwur) of the assented issues, not on the act of assenting.
Look for the phrase 'يتوقف على تصوّر تلك التصديقات - لا على نفسها'.
3
Understand the implication
This means that when we mentally consider the issues (e.g., 'The world is created', 'The Creator is one'), we form concepts of those propositions as objects of thought. These concepts are not themselves acts of assent, but the mental material from which the concept of the science is built.
Thus, tasawwur still precedes tasdiq in the order of knowing: we must first conceptualize the propositions before we assent to them.
4
Apply to an example
Take the science of kalam. Its issues include 'the world is created'. Assenting to that proposition is tasdiq. But before assenting, we have a concept of 'the world' and 'created'. The concept of the science of kalam is built from such concepts, not from the assents themselves. This preserves the logical order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماهية وحقيقة وراء تلك المسائل، فمعرفته بحسب حده وحقيقته لا تحصل إلا بالعلم بجميع مسائله، وليس ذلك مقدمة للشروع فيه، إنما المقدمة معرفته بحسب رسمه فلهذا صرّح بقوله : «ورسموه»، دون أن يقول : وحَدُّوه» - إلى غير ذلك من العبارات - تنبيهاً على أن مقدمة الشروع في كل علم رسمه، لاحده. فإن قلت : العلم بالمسائل هو التصديق بها ومعرفة العلم بحده تصور، والتصور لا يستفاد من التصديق. قلت : العلم بالمسائل هو التصديق بالمسائل حتى إذا حصل التصديق بجميع المسائل حصل العلم المطلوب، لكن تصوّر العلم المطلوب محله يتوقف على تصوّر تلك التصديقات - لا على نفسها - فالتصور غير مستفاد من التصديق.
Essence and reality are beyond those issues, so knowledge of it according to its definition (hadd) and reality is not obtained except by knowing all its issues, and that is not a preface for starting into it. Rather, the preface is knowing it according to its description (rasm). For this reason he explicitly said 'and they described it', instead of saying 'and they defined it (hadd)' – among other expressions – as a notification that the preface for starting any science is its description (rasm), not its definition (hadd). For if you say: Knowledge of the issues is assent (tasdiq) to them, and knowledge of the science by its definition is a concept (tasawwur), and a concept is not derived from assent. I say: Knowledge of the issues is assent to the issues, so if assent to all issues is obtained, the desired science is obtained. But the conceptualization of the desired science – i.e., its definition – depends on the conceptualization of those assents – not on the assents themselves – so the concept is not derived from the assent.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage continues the explanation of why the definition of a science given at the beginning is a rasm, not a hadd. The author argues that to know the essence (hadd) of a science requires knowing all its issues, which is impossible before studying it. Therefore, the introductory definition must be a rasm, i.e., based on external or descriptive features. He notes that the author of the matn deliberately used 'rasamooh' (they described it) instead of 'haddooh' (they defined it) to emphasize this point. Then an objection is raised: knowledge of the issues is assent (tasdiq), while knowledge of the definition is a concept (tasawwur). If the definition depends on knowing all issues, then the concept would be derived from assent, which is impossible because tasawwur is prior to tasdiq. The author's answer distinguishes: the concept of the science itself is not derived from the assents themselves, but from the conceptualization (tasawwur) of those assents. In other words, knowing the issues as a mental object provides the material for the concept of the science, not the assents directly. This preserves the epistemological order: tasawwur precedes tasdiq.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
مسائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, on what does the conceptualization (tasawwur) of a science depend?
A) On the assents (tasdiq) to its issues
B) On the conceptualization of those assents
C) On the hadd of the science
D) On the rasm alone

Key Takeaway
The concept of a science is derived from conceptualizing its issues, not from assenting to them, preserving the priority of tasawwur over tasdiq.

Defining Qawl Sharh and Hujja
Author defines the two main parts of logic: qawl sharh and hujja.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qawl sharh and hujja based on their functions in the logical process.

Core Idea
Definitions lead to conceptualization; proofs lead to assent.

This passage introduces two fundamental terms in Islamic logic: the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) and the proof (hujja). Understanding what each term means and what it accomplishes is essential for navigating any logical text, as these two operations form the backbone of the discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate the words 'qawl sharh' and 'hujja' in the Arabic. The author explicitly names them as customary designations.
The term 'qawl sharh' literally means 'explanatory statement'.
2
Note what each leads to
Observe that qawl sharh is said to lead to tasawwur (conceptualization), while hujja leads to tasdiq (assent). These are the two cognitive aims of logic.
3
Recognize the conventional naming
The author uses the phrase 'جرت العادة' (it is customary) to indicate that this is an established convention among logicians, not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد جرت العادة بأن يسمّى الموصل إلى التصوّر «قولا شارحا» والموصل إلى التصديق «حجّة» ويجب تقديم الأوّل على الثاني وضعاً، لتقدم التصوّر على التصديق طبعاً، لأنّ كلّ تصديق لابد فيه من تصوّر المحكوم عليه، إما بذاته أو بأمر صادق عليه، والمحكوم به كذلك والحكم - لامتناع الحكم مع جهل أحد هذه الأمور.
He said: And it is customary to call the thing that leads to conceptualization an 'explanatory statement' (qawl shāriḥ) and the thing that leads to assent a 'proof' (ḥujja). And the first must be placed before the second by convention, because conceptualization naturally precedes assent, for every assent necessarily involves conceptualization of the thing judged, either in itself or by something true of it, and likewise the thing judged by it, and the judgment itself – since judgment is impossible with ignorance of any of these things.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage introduces two key technical terms: 'qawl sharh' (explanatory statement) for what leads to conceptualization (i.e., definition), and 'ḥujja' (proof) for what leads to assent (i.e., argument). The text then asserts that the qawl sharh must be presented before the ḥujja because conceptualization naturally precedes assent. The reason: every act of assent requires prior conceptualization of the subject, the predicate, and the relation (judgment) between them; without conceptualization, judgment is impossible. This establishes the standard order in logical texts: definitions first, then arguments.

Key Terms
القول الشارح (al-qawl al-shāriḥ): The explanatory statement (definition or description) that leads from known conceptuals to an unknown conceptual. [Note: No significant shift; this is standard terminology.]
الحجة (al-ḥujja): The proof or argument that leads from known assents to an unknown assent. [Note: In later texts, especially in jadal, ḥujja may refer specifically to a demonstrative proof, but here it is broad.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Conceptualization (tasawwur)
C) Judgment (hukm)
D) Proof (hujja)

Key Takeaway
The qawl sharh serves to produce conceptualization, while the hujja produces assent; this functional distinction is foundational in Islamic logic.

Why Definition Precedes Proof
Author explains why definition precedes proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why definition (qawl sharh) must precede proof (hujja) by analyzing the relationship between conceptualization and assent.

Core Idea
Assent requires prior conceptualization; without it, judgment is impossible.

The same passage that defines qawl sharh and hujja also gives a compelling reason for their order: definition must come before proof because conceptualization naturally precedes assent. Let us unpack this reasoning step by step, as it justifies the entire structure of logical instruction.

Guided Path
1
State the order claim
The author says 'the first must be placed before the second' (يجب تقديم الأوّل على الثاني). Identify that 'the first' refers to qawl sharh and 'the second' to hujja.
2
Understand the natural precedence
The reason given is 'because conceptualization naturally precedes assent' (لتقدم التصوّر على التصديق طبعاً). This is a psychological or epistemological fact: you must have a concept before you can affirm or deny something about it.
3
Identify the three necessary conceptualizations
The author lists three things that must be conceptualized for any judgment: the subject of the judgment (المحكوم عليه), the predicate (المحكوم به), and the judgment itself (الحكم). Without conceptualization of any of these, judgment is impossible.
4
See the impossibility of judgment without conceptualization
Consider a concrete example: to judge 'the world is created', you must have a concept of 'world' (subject), 'created' (predicate), and the relation of predication. If you are ignorant of any, you cannot make the judgment.
5
Apply to logical order
Since hujja aims to produce assent, which requires judgment, and judgment requires prior conceptualization (provided by qawl sharh), the qawl sharh must logically come first in any systematic presentation.

Source Text

And the first must be placed before the second by convention, because conceptualization naturally precedes assent, for every assent necessarily involves conceptualization of the thing judged, either in itself or by something true of it, and likewise the thing judged by it, and the judgment itself – since judgment is impossible with ignorance of any of these things.

The Two Divisions of Logic: Qawl Sharh and Hujja
Author defines qawl sharh and hujja as the two divisions of logic and explains their necessary order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qawl sharh and hujja and explain why qawl sharj must precede hujja in logical arrangement.

Core Idea
Logic is about two doors: conceptualization and assent; each has its own key.

This passage lays the foundation of the entire discipline of logic: it divides all knowledge into two kinds, and then assigns to each kind a specific instrument. Understanding this division is essential before engaging with any logical argument, because without clear concepts, no proof can even be stated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of knowledge
The text states that knowledge is either tasawwur (conceptualization) or tasdiq (assent). Recognize that all known and unknown things fall into these two categories.
Tasawwur is grasping the meaning without judgment; tasdiq is affirming or denying something.
2
Define the instruments for each kind
Find where the author says what conveys to tasawwur is called a qawl sharh, and what conveys to tasdiq is called a hujja. Note the conventional naming.
Qawl sharh is an explanatory statement; hujja is a proof.
3
Understand the natural precedence
The author argues that qawl sharh must come before hujja in arrangement (wad'an) because tasawwur naturally precedes tasdiq (tab'an). Locate the phrase 'li-taqaddum al-tasawwur ala al-tasdiq tab'an'.
Natural precedence means one thing is a prerequisite for the other by nature.
4
Analyze the justification
Every assent (tasdiq) necessarily involves a conceptualization of the subject, predicate, and judgment. Without these concepts, judgment is impossible. This proves why conceptualization must come first.
The text mentions 'al-mahkum alayh', 'al-mahkum bih', and 'al-hukm'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الشارح والحجة]
قال : وقد جرت العادة بأن يسمّى الموصل إلى التصوّر «قولا شارحاً» والموصل إلى التصديق «حجّة» ويجب تقديم الأوّل على الثاني وضعاً، لتقدم التصوّر على التصديق طبعاً، لأنّ كلّ تصديق لابد فيه من تصوّر المحكوم عليه، إما بذاته أو بأمر صادق عليه، والمحكوم به كذلك والحكم - لامتناع الحكم معمن جهل أحد هذه الأمور.
(1) لما انحصر العلم في التصوّر والتصديق انحصر المعلوم في المتصور والمصدق به قطعاً، وانحصر المجهول أيضاً في التصوري والتصديقي، لأن ما كان مجهولاً إما أن يكون بحيث إذا علم وأدرك كان إدراكه تصوراً، وإما أن يكون بحيث إذا علم وأدرك كان إدراكه تصديقاً (شريف). (۲) وذلك لأن الحد التام مركب قطعاً، والحد الناقص قد يكون مركباً وقد لا يكون - عند من جوز الحد الناقص بالفصل وحده - والرسم التام مركب قطعاً، والرسم الناقص قديكون مركباً وقد لا يكون - عند من جوز الرسم الناقص بالخاصة وحدها.
فإن قلت : القول الشارح موصل إلى التصور بطريق النظر، وقد تقدم أن النظر ترتيب أمور معلومة، فكيف يجوز أن يكون القول الشارح غير مركب ؟ قلت : من جوز الحد الناقص بالفصل وحده والرسم الناقص بالخاصة وحدها قال في تعريف النظر : «إنّه تحصيل أمر أو ترتيب أمور» لكن المصنف قد تسامح فاعتبر في النظر الترتيب، وجوز التعريف بالفصل وحده، وبالخاصة وحدها (شريف).
[The Explanatory Statement and the Proof]
He said: It is customary that what conveys to conceptualization is called an ‘explanatory statement’ (qawl sharh), and what conveys to assent is called a ‘proof’ (hujja). It is necessary to place the first before the second in arrangement, because conceptualization naturally precedes assent; for every assent necessarily involves a conceptualization of the subject of judgment, either in itself or by something true of it, and similarly of the predicate and the judgment—since judgment is impossible when one is ignorant of these things.
(1) Since knowledge is confined to conceptualization and assent, the known is necessarily confined to the conceptualized and the assented to, and the unknown is also confined to the conceptual and the assertive; because whatever is unknown is either such that when known it is a conceptualization, or such that when known it is an assent (Sharif). (2) That is because the complete definition is certainly composite, and the incomplete definition may be composite or not—according to those who allow the incomplete definition as the differentia alone—and the complete description is certainly composite, and the incomplete description may be composite or not—according to those who allow the incomplete description as the property alone.
If you say: The explanatory statement conveys to conceptualization via theoretical reflection (nazar), and it has been stated that theoretical reflection is an arrangement of known things; so how can the explanatory statement be non-composite? I say: Those who allow the incomplete definition as the differentia alone and the incomplete description as the property alone said concerning the definition of theoretical reflection: ‘It is the acquisition of a matter or the arrangement of things’; but the author (al-Qazwini) was lax and considered theoretical reflection to be an arrangement, and he allowed definition by the differentia alone and by the property alone (Sharif).
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the two main parts of logic: the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) and the proof (hujja). The author states that it is customary to call what leads to conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) an 'explanatory statement', and what leads to assertive knowledge (tasdiq) a 'proof'. The explanatory statement is so named because it is typically composite (murakkab), and it explains and clarifies the essences of things. The proof is so named because the one who adheres to it for arguing his claim overcomes the opponent (from hajja = to overcome). The author then stresses that the explanatory statement must precede the proof in arrangement, because conceptualization naturally precedes assent: every assent requires conceptualization of the subject, predicate, and judgment. The footnotes expand on these points: (1) since knowledge is limited to tasawwur and tasdiq, the unknown is also of these two types; (2) discussion of why the explanatory statement is often composite: complete definitions and descriptions are composite, while incomplete ones may be non-composite if they consist of a single differentia or property. The passage ends with a question-and-answer: if the explanatory statement leads to conceptualization through theoretical reflection (nazar), and nazar is a sequence of known items, how can a non-composite statement be explanatory? The answer is that those who allow non-composite definitions (e.g., by differentia alone) define nazar more broadly as 'the acquisition of one thing or a sequence of things', thus allowing a single term to be an explanatory statement.

Key Terms
قول شارح
حجة
تقدم طبعا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because conceptualization naturally precedes assent
C) Because the explanatory statement is always simple
D) Because the proof relies on demonstration

Key Takeaway
The logical order of knowledge demands that we first grasp concepts (tasawwur) before we can affirm or deny (tasdiq); therefore, the study of explanatory statements precedes that of proofs.

The Four Types of Simple Expressions (al-Mufrad)
Author defines the simple expression and lists its four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any expression as simple or compound by applying the four-type classification.

Core Idea
The simple is defined by intention, not by absence of parts.

The passage begins by defining the simple expression in a comprehensive way, listing four distinct cases that all satisfy the core criterion: no part of the expression is intended to signify a part of its meaning. This classification is foundational for understanding how terms function in logic and Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the core criterion
Read the definition: a simple expression is one where no part is intended to signify a part of its meaning. This intention-based approach is key.
2
Examine the four categories
The author lists four cases: (1) no parts at all, (2) parts with no meaning, (3) parts with meaning not part of intended meaning, (4) parts signifying part of meaning but not intended.
The fourth case is subtle: the part's signification is not the speaker's intention.
3
Apply to examples
Test 'Zayd' (type 1), 'Abdullah' (type 3), and 'writer' (compound). Understand why each falls where it does.
'Abdullah' is simple because the composite meaning is not intended; it's a name.
4
Test a borderline case
Consider 'pencil sharpener' – each part is intended to contribute to the overall meaning, so it is compound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاً - أي وإن لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه – فهو المفرد، سواء لم يكن له جزء أو كان له جزء ولم يدلّ على معنى، أو كان له جزء دال على معنى ولا يكون ذلك المعنى جزء المعنى المقصود من اللفظ كـ «عبد الله » ، أو كان له جزء دال على جزء المعنى المقصود لكن لم يكن دلالته مقصودة ؛ فحدّ المفرد يتناول الألفاظ الأربعة.
فإن قلت : المفرد مقدم على المركب طبعاً، فلم أخره وضعاً، ومخالفة الوضع الطبع في قوة الخطإ عند المحصلين.
فنقول : للمفرد والمركب اعتباران : أحدهما بحسب الذات، وهو ماصدق عليه المفرد من زيد وعمرو ،وغيرهما وثانيهما بحسب المفهوم وهو ما وضع اللفظ بإزائه كالكاتب - مثلاً - فإن له مفهوماً هو شيء له الكتابة، وذاتاً هو ماصدق عليه الكاتب من أفراد الإنسان.
فإن عنيتم بقولكم : «المفرد مقدّم على المركب طبعاً» أن ذات المفرد مقدم على ذات المركب، فمسلّم، ولكن تأخيره هاهنا في التعريف، والتعريف ليس بحسب الذات بل بحسب المفهوم.
وإن عنيتم به أن مفهوم المفرد مقدم على مفهوم المركب، فهو ممنوع؛ فإن القيود في مفهوم المركب وجوديّة، وفي مفهوم المفرد عدمية، والوجود في التصوّر سابق على العدم، فلذا أخر المفرد في التعريف وقدمه في الأقسام والأحكام لأنها بحسب الذات.
And the first: if no part of it is intended to indicate part of its meaning, then it is the simple (mufrad), whether it has no part, or has a part and does not indicate a meaning, or has a part indicating a meaning and that meaning is not part of the intended meaning of the expression like 'Abdullah', or has a part indicating part of the intended meaning but its indication is not intended. So the definition of the simple encompasses these four types of expressions. If you say: the simple is naturally prior to the compound, so why did you delay it in the exposition, and opposing natural order is close to error in the view of verifiers? We say: the simple and compound have two aspects: one according to essence, which is what the simple applies to like Zayd and Amr and others, and the second according to concept, which is what the expression is conventionally correlated with – for example 'writer' – for it has a concept which is 'something having writing', and an essence which is the individuals of man to which 'writer' applies. If by your statement 'the simple is naturally prior to the compound' you mean that the essence of the simple is prior to the essence of the compound, then it is granted, but its delay here is in the definition, and the definition is not according to essence but according to concept. And if you mean that the concept of the simple is prior to the concept of the compound, then it is denied; for the restrictions in the concept of the compound are positive, and in the concept of the simple are negative, and existence in conceptualization precedes non-existence, so the simple is delayed in the definition and prioritized in the divisions and rulings because they are according to essence.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the simple expression and a response to an objection regarding its priority. The author first restates the definition: a simple expression is one where no part of it is intended to signify a part of its meaning. He then enumerates four cases covered by this definition: (1) the expression has no parts at all, (2) it has parts but they do not signify any meaning, (3) it has a part that signifies a meaning but that meaning is not part of the intended meaning of the whole (e.g., 'Abdullah' where 'Abd' and 'Allah' each have meaning but the whole does not intend a part of its meaning from those parts), and (4) it has a part that signifies a part of the intended meaning but the signification of that part is not intended. The objection states that the simple is naturally prior to the compound, so why has the author placed the compound first in his exposition? To answer, the author distinguishes two aspects of the simple and compound: essence (what the term applies to in external reality) and concept (the mental meaning correlated with the term). If 'natural priority' refers to essence, then it is true that the essence of simple things is prior, but the definition is concerned with concepts, not essences. If it refers to concepts, then the concept of the compound is actually prior because it involves positive restrictions, while the concept of the simple involves negation (lack of composition). Since existence (positive restrictions) is prior in mental conceptualization, the compound's concept is more readily understood, so it is appropriate to define the compound first. However, when dealing with divisions and rulings that pertain to external essences, the simple is prioritized, consistent with its essential priority.

Key Terms
المفرد
المركب
الذات
المفهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four types of simple expressions mentioned in the passage?
A) Expression with no parts
B) Expression with parts that do not indicate any meaning
C) Expression with parts that indicate part of the meaning but that indication is intended
D) Expression with parts that indicate a meaning not part of the intended meaning

Key Takeaway
A simple expression is defined by the intention that no part signifies a part of its meaning, not by an absence of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which type of simple 'al-Rahman' (the Merciful) is. Is it simple or compound? Justify.
?
Find an expression in the Qur'an that is compound and explain why it is compound based on the four-type classification.

Resolving the Priority Objection: Essence vs Concept
Author rebuts an objection about the order of definition using the essence-concept distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve apparent contradictions in logical exposition by applying the distinction between essence and concept.

Core Idea
The simple is naturally prior in essence but conceptually posterior — so defining compound first is justified.

The author anticipates an objection: if the simple is naturally prior, why define the compound first? He answers by introducing a distinction that is fundamental in Islamic logic: every term has an essence (the external reality it applies to) and a concept (the mental meaning). This distinction not only resolves the objection but also illuminates the difference between definitions and divisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector argues that the simple is naturally prior to the compound, so it should be defined first. Violating this order is almost an error.
2
Understand the two aspects
The author introduces 'essence' (the individuals the term applies to) and 'concept' (the mental meaning). Examples: 'writer' has a concept 'something that writes' and an essence 'individual humans who write'.
3
Analyze the response
If priority refers to essence, it is granted but irrelevant because definitions concern concepts. If priority refers to concepts, the compound is actually prior because its concept involves positive restrictions which are mentally prior to negative restrictions (the simple's concept is negative: lack of composition).
Positive restrictions are easier to conceptualize than negative ones.
4
Apply the resolution
Thus, the simple is delayed in definition but prioritized in divisions and rulings because those deal with essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاً - أي وإن لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه – فهو المفرد، سواء لم يكن له جزء أو كان له جزء ولم يدلّ على معنى، أو كان له جزء دال على معنى ولا يكون ذلك المعنى جزء المعنى المقصود من اللفظ كـ «عبد الله » ، أو كان له جزء دال على جزء المعنى المقصود لكن لم يكن دلالته مقصودة ؛ فحدّ المفرد يتناول الألفاظ الأربعة.
فإن قلت : المفرد مقدم على المركب طبعاً، فلم أخره وضعاً، ومخالفة الوضع الطبع في قوة الخطإ عند المحصلين.
فنقول : للمفرد والمركب اعتباران : أحدهما بحسب الذات، وهو ماصدق عليه المفرد من زيد وعمرو ،وغيرهما وثانيهما بحسب المفهوم وهو ما وضع اللفظ بإزائه كالكاتب - مثلاً - فإن له مفهوماً هو شيء له الكتابة، وذاتاً هو ماصدق عليه الكاتب من أفراد الإنسان.
فإن عنيتم بقولكم : «المفرد مقدّم على المركب طبعاً» أن ذات المفرد مقدم على ذات المركب، فمسلّم، ولكن تأخيره هاهنا في التعريف، والتعريف ليس بحسب الذات بل بحسب المفهوم.
وإن عنيتم به أن مفهوم المفرد مقدم على مفهوم المركب، فهو ممنوع؛ فإن القيود في مفهوم المركب وجوديّة، وفي مفهوم المفرد عدمية، والوجود في التصوّر سابق على العدم، فلذا أخر المفرد في التعريف وقدمه في الأقسام والأحكام لأنها بحسب الذات.
And the first: if no part of it is intended to indicate part of its meaning, then it is the simple (mufrad), whether it has no part, or has a part and does not indicate a meaning, or has a part indicating a meaning and that meaning is not part of the intended meaning of the expression like 'Abdullah', or has a part indicating part of the intended meaning but its indication is not intended. So the definition of the simple encompasses these four types of expressions. If you say: the simple is naturally prior to the compound, so why did you delay it in the exposition, and opposing natural order is close to error in the view of verifiers? We say: the simple and compound have two aspects: one according to essence, which is what the simple applies to like Zayd and Amr and others, and the second according to concept, which is what the expression is conventionally correlated with – for example 'writer' – for it has a concept which is 'something having writing', and an essence which is the individuals of man to which 'writer' applies. If by your statement 'the simple is naturally prior to the compound' you mean that the essence of the simple is prior to the essence of the compound, then it is granted, but its delay here is in the definition, and the definition is not according to essence but according to concept. And if you mean that the concept of the simple is prior to the concept of the compound, then it is denied; for the restrictions in the concept of the compound are positive, and in the concept of the simple are negative, and existence in conceptualization precedes non-existence, so the simple is delayed in the definition and prioritized in the divisions and rulings because they are according to essence.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the simple expression and a response to an objection regarding its priority. The author first restates the definition: a simple expression is one where no part of it is intended to signify a part of its meaning. He then enumerates four cases covered by this definition: (1) the expression has no parts at all, (2) it has parts but they do not signify any meaning, (3) it has a part that signifies a meaning but that meaning is not part of the intended meaning of the whole (e.g., 'Abdullah' where 'Abd' and 'Allah' each have meaning but the whole does not intend a part of its meaning from those parts), and (4) it has a part that signifies a part of the intended meaning but the signification of that part is not intended. The objection states that the simple is naturally prior to the compound, so why has the author placed the compound first in his exposition? To answer, the author distinguishes two aspects of the simple and compound: essence (what the term applies to in external reality) and concept (the mental meaning correlated with the term). If 'natural priority' refers to essence, then it is true that the essence of simple things is prior, but the definition is concerned with concepts, not essences. If it refers to concepts, then the concept of the compound is actually prior because it involves positive restrictions, while the concept of the simple involves negation (lack of composition). Since existence (positive restrictions) is prior in mental conceptualization, the compound's concept is more readily understood, so it is appropriate to define the compound first. However, when dealing with divisions and rulings that pertain to external essences, the simple is prioritized, consistent with its essential priority.

Key Terms
المفرد
المركب
الذات
المفهوم

Key Takeaway
The apparent conflict between natural priority and definitional order is resolved by distinguishing essence (what the term applies to) from concept (its mental meaning).

Why Only Correspondence Signification Determines Composition
Author explains why only correspondence signification is considered in the division and responds to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why only correspondence signification (dalalat al-mutabaqa) is used to determine whether an expression is simple or compound, and identify the problem that would arise if inclusive or implicative signification were also considered.

Core Idea
Inclusive and implicative signification are excluded because they would allow the same expression to be both simple and compound in the same context, creating confusion.

In this passage, the author explains why the division of expressions into simple and compound relies solely on correspondence signification (dalalat al-mutabaqa). He argues that including inclusive (tadammun) or implicative (iltizam) signification would lead to logical problems, because an expression could be simple by correspondence but compound by inclusion simultaneously. An objection and response clarify why this is more problematic than the double classification allowed for expressions like 'Abdullah'. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning and the dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divided term and the three types of signification
The term being divided is 'expression' (لفظ). The three types of signification are: correspondence (مطابقة), inclusion (تضمن), and implication (التزام). The author focuses on why only correspondence is relevant for determining whether an expression is simple (مفرد) or compound (مركب).
2
Understand the criterion for simplicity and compoundness
An expression is compound if a part of it indicates a part of its meaning; it is simple if no part does so. The key point is that this indication must be by correspondence, not by inclusion or implication.
For example, 'human' is compound because 'man' indicates 'rational animal' by correspondence.
3
Trace the problem with considering inclusion or implication
If inclusion were considered, then in a word like 'human', the part 'man' might indicate 'animal' by inclusion (since animal is included in human). Thus, 'human' would be compound by inclusion but simple by correspondence. The same expression would be both simple and compound in the same convention.
This is the crux: the same expression would have contradictory classifications.
4
Evaluate the objection and the response
The objector argues that there is no harm, citing 'Abdullah' which is simple in one convention and compound in another. The responder (al-Sharif) distinguishes: in 'Abdullah', the two classifications occur in different states/conventions, so no confusion. But here it is the same state and convention, causing excessive confusion (التباس).
Thus, restricting to correspondence avoids this confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما اعتبر في المقسم دلالة المطابقة) - لا التضمن ولا الالتزام - لأن المعتبر في تركيب اللفظ وإفراده دلالة جزئه على جزء معناه المطابقي وعدم دلالته عليه، لا دلالة جزئه على جزء معناه التضمني والالتزامي دلالته عليه، فإنه لو اعتبر التضمّن أو الالتزام في التركيب و الإفراد وعدم (١) أي اعتبر في المقسم المطابقة وحدها، ولم يعتبر الدلالة مطلقاً بحيث يندرج فيها التضمن والالتزام أيضاً. وأمّا اعتبار التضمن والالتزام بدون المطابقة فمما لا يذهب إليه وهم. ثم إذا اعتبر مطلق الدلالة فإما أن يشترط في التركيب دلالة جزء اللفظ على جزء معناه المطابقي وجزء معناه التضمّني وجزء معناه الالتزامي جميعاً، حتى إذا قصد بجزء اللفظ الدلالة على أجزاء معانيه الثلاثة كان مركباً وإذا انتفى الدلالات الثلاث بالقياس إلى أجزاء جميع هذه المعاني أو بالقياس إلى بعضها كان مفرداً، وإما أن يكتفي في التركيب بالدلالة على جزء من أجزاء هذه المعاني، وحينئذ يتحقق التركيب بالنظر إلى المطابقة وحدها وبالنظر إلى غيرها أيضاً. وكذلك يتحقق الإفراد بالنظر إلى كل واحدة من الدلالات الثلاث، لأنه عدم التركيب، فإذا انتفى التركيب نظراً إلى التضمن - مثلاً - كان هناك إفراد نظراً إليه ؛ والأوّل مستبعد جداً، فلذلك لم يتعرض له، وبين أن الثاني يستلزم كون اللفظ مفرداً مركباً معاً نظراً إلى دلالتين. واعترض عليه بأنه لا محذور في ذلك، بل هو أولى بالجواز مما جوزوه من تركيب اللفظ وإفراده نظراً إلى معنيين مطابقيّين. وقد يعتذر عن ذلك بأن التركيب والإفراد في «عبد الله إنما كانا في حالتين وبحسب وضعين مختلفين، فليس هناك زيادة التباس بين الأقسام، بخلاف مانحن فيه، فإن التركيب والإفراد فيه وإن كانا باعتبار دلالتين، لكنهما في حالة واحدة وبحسب وضع واحد؛ فتلتبس الأقسام زيادة التباس (شريف).
And the reason only correspondence signification (dalalat al-mutabaqa) is considered in the divided term – not inclusion or implication – is that what is considered in the composition and simplicity of an expression is the indication of its part upon a part of its correspondence meaning or the lack thereof, not the indication of its part upon a part of its inclusive or implicative meaning. For if inclusion or implication were considered in composition and simplicity and non-[composition], then ... (1) i.e., only correspondence is considered in the divided term, and signification absolutely is not considered such that inclusion and implication are also included. And considering inclusion and implication without correspondence is something no one would imagine. Then if absolute signification is considered, either it is required in composition that the part of the expression indicates a part of its correspondence meaning and a part of its inclusive meaning and a part of its implicative meaning all together, so that if the part of the expression is intended to indicate the parts of its three meanings, it is compound, and if the three indications are absent with respect to the parts of all these meanings or with respect to some, it is simple; or it suffices in composition for the indication of a part of these meanings, and then composition is realized with respect to correspondence alone and with respect to other [types] as well. Similarly, simplicity is realized with respect to each of the three significations, because it is the absence of composition, so if composition is absent with respect to inclusion, for example, then there is simplicity with respect to it. The first [option] is very far-fetched, so he did not address it, and he showed that the second entails that the expression is both simple and compound together with respect to two significations. It was objected that there is no harm in that; indeed it is more permissible than what they allowed of composition and simplicity of an expression with respect to two correspondence meanings. And one may excuse that composition and simplicity in 'Abdullah' were in two states and according to two different conventions, so there is no additional confusion between the divisions, unlike our case, where composition and simplicity, even if by virtue of two significations, are in one state and according to one convention, so the divisions become more confused (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that explains why the division of expressions into simple and compound is based solely on correspondence signification (dalalat al-mutabaqa), excluding inclusive and implicative signification. The reason is that composition and simplicity are determined by whether a part signifies a part of the correspondence meaning of the whole, not by other types of signification. If we were to consider inclusion or implication, it would lead to problems. The commentator then explores two possibilities if we were to consider absolute signification (including all three types): either we require that for composition, the part must indicate parts of all three meanings – which is far-fetched and not discussed – or we allow composition if the part indicates any part of any of the three meanings. In the latter case, the same expression could be both simple and compound relative to different significations (e.g., simple by correspondence but compound by inclusion). An objection is raised: there is no harm in that; it is even more permissible than allowing an expression to be both simple and compound with respect to two different correspondence meanings (as in 'Abdullah' which is simple in one convention and compound in another). The commentator responds with an excuse from al-Sharif: in the case of 'Abdullah', the simplicity and composition occur in two different states and conventions, so there is no confusion. But in our scenario, the expression would be both simple and compound in the same state and convention, leading to excessive confusion between the divisions. Therefore, it is better to restrict the division to correspondence signification only.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
التضمن
الالتزام
المقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because only correspondence signification is real; inclusion and implication are metaphorical.
D) Because the logicians unanimously agreed to do so.

Key Takeaway
Only correspondence signification (dalalat al-mutabaqa) determines whether an expression is simple or compound, because including other types would create contradictory classifications in the same context.

The Verb's Form as the Indicator of Tense
Author defines kalima (verb) and explains why its form indicates tense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hay'a (form) and madda (matter) of any Arabic verb and explain how the form indicates tense.

Core Idea
The verb's tense comes from its form, not its root.

In logic, 'kalima' means verb, defined as a word that indicates an event and is distinguished by its form indicating tense. This lesson unpacks that definition, showing how the morphological form (hay'a) is the primary carrier of temporal meaning, separate from the root letters (madda). We will work through the definition, test it against examples, and address objections raised by later commentators.

Guided Path
1
Separate hay'a from madda in a given verb
Take a verb like 'ضَرَبَ' (daraba). The root letters ض-ر-ب form the madda (matter). The pattern of vowels and the order of letters (here فَعَلَ) is the hay'a (form). Identify both components.
2
Compare verbs with same root but different tense
Compare 'ضَرَبَ' (past) and 'يَضْرِبُ' (present). They share the same root ض-ر-ب, so the madda is identical. The forms differ (فَعَلَ vs يَفْعِلُ). The change in tense corresponds to the change in form, showing form indicates tense.
3
Compare verbs with same form but different roots
Compare 'ضَرَبَ' (daraba, hit) and 'طَلَبَ' (talaba, seek). Both have the form فَعَلَ and both indicate past tense. The roots differ, but tense is the same. This supports that form, not root, determines tense.
4
Test against objections: non-Arabic languages and active/passive forms
Objection: In Persian, 'آمد' (amad, came) and 'آید' (ayad, comes) share the same form in writing but differ in tense. Response: The definition applies primarily to Arabic. Another objection: active vs passive past forms (ضَرَبَ vs ضُرِبَ) have different forms but same past tense. Counter: The form still indicates past in a general sense; the specific time (past) is constant.
The commentator (Sharif) provides these responses to defend the definition.
5
Explain why the verb is not composite despite two components
If madda indicates the event and hay'a indicates tense, one might think the verb is composite. The author clarifies that true composition requires temporal order of parts in speech. Here, the madda and hay'a are simultaneous and not heard sequentially, so the verb is not composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالهيأة والصيغة الهيأة الحاصلة للحروف باعتبار تقديمها وتأخيرها وحركاتها وسكناتها، وهي صورة الكلمة، والحروف مادتها. وإنما قيّد حدّ الكلمة بها لإخراج ما يدل على الزمان لا بهيأته – بل بحسب جوهره ومادته - كالزمان والأمس واليوم والصبوح والغبق فإن دلالتها على الزمان بموادها وجواهرها لا بهيئاتها، بخلاف الكلمات فإن دلالتها على الزمان بحسب هيأتها، بشهادة اختلاف الزمان عند اختلاف الهيأة وإن اتحدت المادة كضرب ويضرب؛ فاتحاد الزمان عند اتحاد الهيأة وإن اختلفت المادة، كضرب وطلب. لم يرد بذلك أن الجوهر وحده دالّ على تلك الأزمنة، حتى يرد أنه يلزم من ذلك أن يكون تقاليب الزمان بأسرها دالة على ما يدل عليه لفظ الزمان - وهو باطل قطعاً - بل أراد أن الجوهر له مدخل ما في الدلالة على الزمان، بخلاف الكلمة، فإن الهيأة هناك مستقلة بالدلالة على الزمان - كما سيذكره. واعترض عليه بأن دلالة الكلمة على الزمان بالصيغة - أن صحت - فإنما تصح في لغة العرب دون لغة العجم، فإن قولك: «آمد» و«آيد» متحدان في الصيغة ومختلفان في الزمان، وقد تقدّم أن نظر الفن في الألفاظ على وجه كلّي غير مخصوص بلغة دون أخرى. وأجيب بأن الاهتمام باللغة العربية - التي دون بها الفنّ غالباً في زماننا أكثر - فلا بعد في اختصاص بعض الأحوال بهذه اللغة - كما مرت إليه الإشارة (شريف). رد عليه بأن صيغ الماضي - في التكلم والخطاب والغيبة - مختلفة قطعاً، ولا اختلاف في الزمان؛ بل نقول: صيغة المجهول من الماضي مخالفة لصيغة المعلوم، وصيغته من الثلاثي المجرد والمزيد والرباعى مختلفة بلا اشتباه، وليس هناك اختلاف زمان، فليس اختلاف الصيغة مستلزماً لاختلاف الزمان حتى يتم شهادته على أن الدال على الزمان هو الصيغة (شريف). فإن قلت: فعلى هذا يلزم أن تكون الكلمة مركبة، لدلالة أصلها ومادتها على الحدث وهيأتها وصورتها على الزمان، فيكون جزؤها دالاً على جزء معناها. فنقول: المعنى من التركيب أن يكون هناك أجزاء مترتبة الألفاظ الثلاث والحروف، والهيأة مع المادة ليست بهذه المثابة (أي مرتبة في السمع بأن يسمع بعضها قبل وبعضها بعد (شريف)).
What is meant by 'hay'a' and 'sigha' is the form that occurs to the letters by virtue of their order, their vowel marks, and their sukoons. It is the form of the word, while the letters are its matter. The definition of 'kalima' is restricted by it to exclude what indicates time not by its form – but by its essence and matter – like 'zaman', 'ams', 'yawm', 'subuh', and 'ghabaq', for their indication of time is by their matters and essences, not by their forms. Unlike verbs, for their indication of time is by their form, as evidenced by the difference in time when the form differs even if the matter is the same, like 'daraba' and 'yadribu'; and the unity of time when the form is united even if the matter differs, like 'daraba' and 'talaba'. [The author] did not mean by that that the essence alone indicates those times, so that it would be objected that then all permutations of time would indicate what the word 'zaman' indicates – which is absolutely false. Rather, he meant that the essence has some input in indicating time, unlike the verb, for the form there is independent in indicating time – as he will mention. And it was objected that the verb's indication of time by its form – if correct – is only correct in the Arabic language, not in non-Arabic languages, for your saying 'amad' and 'ayad' are identical in form but differ in time, and it has been stated that the art's consideration of expressions is universal, not particular to one language without another. It was answered that attention to the Arabic language – in which the art is mostly written in our time – is more, so there is no objection to the specificity of some conditions to this language – as has been alluded to (Sharif). It was also objected that past tense forms – in first person, second person, and third person – are different definitely, yet there is no difference in time; rather we say: the passive form of the past differs from the active form, and its form from the simple triliteral, the augmented, and the quadriliteral differ without confusion, yet there is no difference in time. Thus difference in form does not entail difference in time so that it would be evidence that the indicator of time is the form (Sharif). Then if you say: On this basis, the verb would be composite, since its root and matter indicate the event while its form and shape indicate time, so its part indicates part of its meaning. We say: The meaning of composition is that there are parts ordered in the utterance – the three words and letters – and the form together with the matter is not of this sort (i.e., arranged in audition such that some are heard before and some after (Sharif)).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'kalima' (verb) as understood in logic, distinguishing it from nouns and particles. The author begins by clarifying what is meant by 'hay'a' (form) and 'sigha' (morphological pattern): it is the configuration of letters in terms of order, vowel marks, and sukoons. The letters themselves constitute the 'madda' (matter) of the word. The definition of 'kalima' is restricted by reference to its form because some words indicate time not through their form but through their very essence, e.g., 'zaman' (time) is a noun that means time by its root, not by a pattern. In contrast, verbs like 'daraba' (he hit) and 'yadribu' (he hits) indicate different times (past vs. present) despite sharing the same root letters (ض-ر-ب), showing that the form is the indicator. Conversely, 'daraba' and 'talaba' share the same form but differ in root, yet both indicate past time. The author then addresses an objection: might this imply that the root alone indicates time? No, the root has some input but not independently; the form is the primary indicator for verbs. Another objection concerns non-Arabic languages: in Persian, 'amad' (he came) and 'ayad' (he comes) share the same form but differ in tense, challenging the universality of the claim. The response is that logic is primarily concerned with Arabic, so the definition holds within that scope. A further objection notes that different past tense forms (e.g., active vs. passive) share the same tense, so difference in form does not entail difference in time. The reply is that the form indicates time in a general way; the specific time (past) is constant across those forms. Finally, an objection arises: if the root indicates the event and the form indicates time, then the verb would be composite (like a compound word). The author clarifies that true composition requires temporal ordering of parts in speech, whereas the root and form are simultaneous and not heard sequentially, so the verb is not composite in that sense.

Key Terms
الهيأة (al-hay'a)
المادة (al-madda)
الكلمة (al-kalima)
الصيغة (al-sigha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the logicians, what primarily indicates the tense in the verb 'يَضْرِبُ' (yadribu)?
A) The root letters ض-ر-ب
B) The morphological form (hay'a)
C) The context of the sentence
D) The particle 'ي' at the beginning

Key Takeaway
The verb's tense is indicated by its morphological form, not its root letters, as shown by examples like daraba and yadribu.

Nouns with a Single Meaning: Proper, Univocal, Graded
Author enumerates types of nouns based on meaning: singular, common, ambiguous, transferred.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a noun with a single meaning as 'alam (proper), mutawati' (univocal), or mushakkik (graded) based on specificity and equality of application.

Core Idea
A noun with one meaning can belong to one individual, apply equally to many, or apply with varying intensity.

In this passage from Qutbi's commentary, we learn how to categorize nouns that have a single meaning. The key distinction is whether the meaning is specific to one thing (like 'Zayd'), applies equally to all members of a class (like 'human'), or applies to some members more primarily than others (like 'existence' with respect to God vs. creatures). This classification is fundamental for understanding how words relate to concepts in logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the noun has one or multiple meanings
Read the passage: 'إما أن يكون معناه واحداً أو كثيراً'. Your first task is to determine whether the noun in question has a single meaning or multiple meanings. If it has one meaning, proceed to the next step.
This is the root division; the rest of the classification depends on this.
2
Check if the meaning is specific to one individual
If the meaning is single and refers to a specific individual (like a person's name 'Zayd' or a place 'Mecca'), then it is a 'علم' (proper noun). If not, it is a general concept shared by many.
The passage uses 'تشخص ذلك المعنى' – the meaning becomes individuated.
3
Determine if the concept applies equally or with gradation
For a general concept (not a proper noun), ask: do all individuals share equally in the meaning? If yes (e.g., 'human' applies to every human equally), it is 'متواطئ' (univocal). If some individuals have the meaning more strongly or primarily (e.g., 'existence' applies to God more than to contingent beings), it is 'مشكك' (graded).
The passage gives 'الوجود بالنسبة إلى الواجب والممكن' as an example of مشكك.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Test yourself: classify the word 'العلم' (knowledge). Does it refer to a specific individual? No, it is a concept. Does it apply equally to all instances of knowledge? Yes, any instance of knowledge is equally knowledge. So it is متواطئ. Now try 'السماوات' (the heavens) – is it a proper noun? In the Quranic context, it can be considered a proper name for the cosmos? Actually it is often متواطئ. Classify 'الله' – proper noun.
Use real terms from Islamic sciences to practise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الاسم من حيث معناه. قال : وحينئذ إما أن يكون معناه واحداً أو كثيراً. فإن كان الأوّل فإن تشخص ذلك المعنى سمي «علماً» وإلا فـ«متواطئاً» إن استوت أفراده الذهنية والخارجية فيه كالإنسان والشمس و «مشككاً» إن كان حصوله في البعض أولى وأقدم وأشدّ من الآخر، كالوجود بالنسبة إلى الواجب والممكن. وإن كان الثاني فإن كان وضعه لتلك المعاني على السوية فهو «المشترك» كالعين. وإن لم يكن كذلك، بل وضع لأحدهما أولا ثم نقل إلى الثاني وحينئذ إن ترك موضوعه الأوّل يسمّى لفظاً منقولاً عرفياً» إن كان الناقل هو العرف العام كالدابة - و «شرعيّاً» إن كان الناقل هو الشرع – كالصلاة والصوم - و «اصطلاحياً» إن كان هو العرف الخاص - كاصطلاح النحاة والنظار . وإن لم يترك موضوعه الأول يسمّى بالنسبة إلى المنقول عنه «حقيقة»، و بالنسبة إلى المنقول إليه مجازاً كالأسد بالنسبة إلى الحيوان المفترس والرجل الشجاع
Divisions of the noun according to its meaning. He said: Then it is either that its meaning is one or many. If it is the first, then if that meaning is specific it is called a 'proper noun' (علم), otherwise it is 'univocal' (متواطئ) if its mental and external individuals are equal in it, like 'human' and 'sun', and 'equivocal in degree' (مشكك) if its occurrence in some is more prior, earlier, and stronger than in another, like existence with respect to the Necessary and the possible. If it is the second, then if its coinage is for those meanings equally, it is 'the common' (المشترك), like 'ayn. But if not, rather it was coined for one of them first then transferred to the second, and then if it abandons its first subject it is called a 'customarily transferred' word if the transferrer is common custom, like 'dābba', and 'legally transferred' if the transferrer is the law, like 'prayer' and 'fasting', and 'terminologically transferred' if it is a specific custom, like the terminology of grammarians and theoreticians. And if it does not abandon its first subject, it is called with respect to what it is transferred from a 'literal' (حقيقة), and with respect to what it is transferred to a 'metaphorical' (مجاز), like 'lion' with respect to the predatory animal and the brave man.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of nouns based on whether their meaning is one or many. When the meaning is one, if it designates a specific individual (like 'Zayd') it is a proper noun (علم). If it applies to many individuals equally, it is univocal (متواطئ, e.g., 'human' applies to all humans equally). If the application is not equal but graded (some are stronger than others), it is equivocal in degree (مشكك, e.g., 'existence' applies to God more primarily than to contingent things). When the meaning is multiple, if the word was coined for all meanings simultaneously and equally, it is a common term (المشترك, e.g., 'ayn meaning eye, spring, etc.). If it was originally coined for one meaning then transferred to another, we distinguish whether the original meaning is abandoned or not. If abandoned, the word is 'transferred' (منقول), with subtypes depending on the agent of transfer: general custom (عرفي, like 'dābba' originally meaning a crawling animal then any animal), legal custom (شرعي, like 'prayer' from supplication to the ritual prayer), or specific custom (اصطلاحي, like grammatical terms). If the original meaning is retained alongside the new, the word is 'literal' (حقيقة) regarding the original and 'metaphorical' (مجاز) regarding the new, as 'lion' is literal for the animal and metaphorical for a brave man. This classification is essential for understanding how words relate to their meanings in logic and legal interpretation.

Key Terms
علم
متواطئ
مشكك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a 'مشكك'?
A) زيد (Zayd)
B) الإنسان (human)
C) الوجود (existence)
D) العين (the eye/spring)

Key Takeaway
A noun with one meaning is either a proper noun (علم), univocal (متواطئ), or graded (مشكك), depending on specificity and equality of application.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following terms from Islamic sciences as 'alam, mutawati', or mushakkik: 'الرحمن' (the Most Merciful), 'الكتاب' (the Book, meaning the Quran), 'النور' (light, used for Allah in the Quran and for physical light).
?
Look at the word 'العلم' (knowledge) in the phrase 'العلم نور' (knowledge is light). Is 'العلم' used as علم or متواطئ? Explain your answer.
