Nouns with Multiple Meanings: Homonyms, Transferred, Literal and Metaphorical
Author enumerates types of nouns based on meaning: singular, common, ambiguous, transferred.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a noun with multiple meanings as mushtarak, manqul, haqiqa, or majaz and determine the agent of transfer for manqul.

Core Idea
When a word has multiple meanings, the key issue is whether the meanings were originally intended together or one was derived from another.

This passage continues the classification of nouns to those with multiple meanings. The first division is whether the word was coined for all meanings equally (mushtarak) or for one meaning first and then transferred (manqul). For transferred words, we further distinguish whether the original meaning is abandoned (pure manqul) or retained (haqiqa and majaz). The passage also subcategorizes manqul by the agent of transfer: general custom, Islamic law, or technical terminology. This classification is essential for interpreting texts, especially in usul al-fiqh when deciding whether a Quranic word is used literally or metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the noun has multiple meanings
Based on the passage, if the meaning is 'kathiran' (many), you enter the second branch. Your first step is to confirm that the word indeed has several distinct meanings.
Examples: 'عَيْن' has meanings like eye, spring, spy.
2
Check if the word was coined equally for all meanings
If the word was originally assigned to all its meanings on an equal basis (i.e., no historical priority), it is 'المشترك' (common/homonym). The passage gives 'العين' as an example.
In 'مشترك', no single meaning is more original than the others.
3
If not equally coined, identify the transfer type
If the word was first coined for one meaning and then transferred to another, check if the original meaning was abandoned. If abandoned, it is 'منقول' (transferred). Then identify the agent of transfer: general custom (عرفي), legal custom (شرعي), or technical custom (اصطلاحي). The passage gives 'الدابة' (animal) as عرفي, 'الصلاة' (prayer) as شرعي, and grammatical terms as اصطلاحي.
For منقول شرعي, the transfer is by the Lawgiver (Shari'ah).
4
If original meaning is retained, classify as literal and metaphorical
If the original meaning is still used alongside the new meaning, then the word is 'حقيقة' (literal) with respect to the original, and 'مجاز' (metaphorical) with respect to the new. The passage gives 'أسد' (lion) as literal for the animal and metaphorical for a brave man.
Metaphor requires a relationship (علاقة) between the meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage presents a classification of nouns based on whether their meaning is one or many. When the meaning is one, if it designates a specific individual (like 'Zayd') it is a proper noun (علم). Why the Division of Meaning Applies Only to Nouns
Author argues that only nouns have independent meanings, so the division applies only to them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the logical division of meanings into particular and universal is specific to nouns, using the concepts of independent meaning (ma‘nā mustaqill) and instrumental meaning (ālah).

Core Idea
Only nouns have independent meanings; verbs and particles are mere tools.

In classical Islamic logic, the classification of terms into particular and universal is foundational. But this classification does not apply to all parts of speech. Why are verbs and particles excluded? This passage from Qutb al-Din al-Razi's commentary clarifies the criterion: independence of meaning. A noun like 'Zayd' or 'human' has a meaning that can stand alone as a subject of judgment. In contrast, particles like 'min' and verbs like 'daraba' have meanings that are inherently relational—they serve to connect other concepts. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the logical analysis of terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point being argued
The author states that the division of meaning (into particular/universal, etc.) is specific to the noun. Locate this claim in the Arabic text.
Look for the phrase 'هذه القسمة مخصوصة بالاسم'.
2
Understand the criterion: independent meaning
The author argues that only nouns have an independent meaning (معنى مستقل) that can be described by particularity or universality. This means the meaning can be the subject of a judgment.
Contrast with verbs and particles, which lack this independence.
3
Analyze the case of particles
Particles like 'min' have a meaning that is a tool (آلة) to relate two things. For example, 'the beginning of the journey from Basra'—the particle's meaning is not independently intended but serves as a mirror to relate the journey and Basra.
Therefore, particles cannot be predicates, let alone subjects of judgment.
4
Analyze the case of verbs
A complete verb (like 'daraba') consists of an event (الحدث) and a relation (النسبة) between the event and its agent. The relation is like a particle, making the overall meaning non-independent. Only the event part alone (without the relation) can be predicated; the verb as a whole cannot be a subject or a predicate.
Thus verbs are excluded from the classification of meanings.
5
Conclude the scope
Since only nouns have meanings that are fully independent and can be subjects of judgment, the division of meanings (one/many, particular/universal, etc.) applies only to nouns.
This conclusion is explicitly stated at the beginning and reinforced throughout.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول: هذا إشارة إلى قسمة الاسم بالقياس إلى معناه، فالاسم إما (۱) جعل هذه القسمة مخصوصة بالاسم، لأن انقسام اللفظ إلى الجزئي والكلي إنما هو بحسب اتصاف معناه بالجزئية والكلية ومعنى الاسم «من حيث هو معناه» معنى مستقل صالح للاتصاف بهما، فإن معنى زيد من حيث هو معناه، معنى مستقل يصلح لأن يوصف بالجزئية ويحكم بها عليه؛ وكذا معنى الإنسان يصلح لأن يحكم عليه بالكلية؛ وأما الحرف : فإن معناه – من حيث هو معناه - ليس معنى مستقلا صالحاً لأن يكون محكوماً عليه أصلاً، وذلك لأن معنى «من» مثلا هو ابتداء مخصوص ملحوظ بين السير والبصرة – مثلا – على وجه يكون هو آلة لملاحظتهما ومرآة لتعرف حالهما، فلا يكون بهذا الاعتبار ملحوظاً قصداً، فلا يصلح لأن يكون محكوماً به - فضلاً عن أن يكون محكوماً عليه، وكذا الفعل التام كضرب مثلا يشتمل على حدث كالضرب، وعلى نسبة مخصوصة بينه وبين فاعله وتلك النسبة ملحوظة بينهما على أنها آلة لملاحظتهما على قياس معنى الحرف، وهذا المجموع – أعني الحدث مع النسبة الملحوظة بذلك الاعتبار - معنى غير مستقل بالمفهومية، فلا يصلح لأن يحكم عليه بشيء؛ نعم جزؤه - أعني الحدث وحده - مأخوذ في مفهوم الفعل على أنه مسند إلى شيء آخر، فصار الفعل باعتبار جزء معناه محكوماً به ؛ وأما باعتبار مجموع معناه فلايكون محكوما عليه ولا محكوم به أصلاً، فالفعل إنمات
Statement: This refers to the division of the noun with respect to its meaning. So the noun—(1) this division is made specific to the noun, because the division of the utterance into particular and universal is only according to the description of its meaning by particularity and universality. And the meaning of the noun, 'insofar as it is its meaning', is an independent meaning fit to be described by both. For the meaning of Zayd insofar as it is its meaning is an independent meaning suitable to be described by particularity and to be judged thereby. Likewise the meaning of 'human' is suitable to be judged by universality. As for the particle: its meaning—insofar as it is its meaning—is not an independent meaning suitable to be a subject of judgment at all. That is because the meaning of 'min' for example is a specific beginning noticed between 'journey' and 'Basra'—for example—in such a way that it is a tool for noticing them and a mirror for knowing their state. Thus it is not, in this regard, something noticed intentionally, so it is not fit to be a predicate—let alone a subject. Likewise the complete verb like 'daraba' for example includes an event like hitting, and a specific relation between it and its agent, and that relation is noticed between them as a tool for noticing them on the analogy of the meaning of the particle. And this whole—I mean the event with the relation noticed in that regard—is a meaning not independent in intelligibility, so it is not fit to be judged about anything. Yes, its part—I mean the event alone—is taken in the concept of the verb as being attributed to something else, so the verb by virtue of a part of its meaning becomes a predicate; but by virtue of its total meaning it is neither a subject nor a predicate at all. Thus the verb only...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage explains why the division of meanings (singular, multiple, etc.) is specific to nouns and does not apply to verbs or particles. The author argues that only nouns have fully independent meanings (معنى مستقل) that can serve as the subject of a judgment (محكوم عليه). For example, the proper noun 'Zayd' has a meaning that is independent and can be described as particular. The univocal term 'human' can be described as universal. Particles like 'min' (from) have a relational meaning that is not independently intended; it functions as a tool (آلة) to link two other things (e.g., the journey and Basra). Therefore, it cannot be a subject or even a predicate. Similarly, a verb like 'daraba' (he struck) consists of an event (الحدث) and a relation (النسبة) between the event and its agent. The relation is like a particle in being a mere link, so the overall meaning is not independent. However, the event part alone can be predicated of something, but the verb as a whole (event + relation) cannot be a subject or predicate. Hence, the classification of meanings based on oneness or multiplicity applies only to nouns, whose meanings are sufficiently independent to be so characterized. This nuanced discussion is important for understanding the scope of logical analysis of terms.

Key Terms
معنى مستقل (ma‘nā mustaqill): A meaning that is self-sufficient and can be directly intended as a subject or predicate without needing to relate other concepts. [Note: This concept is standard in Islamic logic; later texts may discuss degrees of independence.]
آلة (ālah): A meaning that serves as a tool to relate other meanings, like particles and relations in verbs. [Note: In later grammar, 'ādāt' are function words; same idea.]
حدث (ḥadath): The event or action component of a verb, which is a part of its meaning. [Note: In Arabic grammar, 'ḥadath' is the abstract event; stable.]
نسبة (nisbah): The relation between the event and the agent in a verb, which is like a particle in being a mere link. [Note: Widely used in logic and grammar; no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the classification of meaning (one/many, etc.) apply only to nouns?
A) Because verbs and particles have no meanings.
B) Because nouns have independent meanings that can be described as particular or universal.
C) Because the tradition arbitrarily restricts it to nouns.
D) Because verbs and particles are not words.

Key Takeaway
The classification of meanings into particular and universal applies only to nouns because only they have independent meanings fit to be subjects of judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify whether the word 'al-‘ālam' (the world) has an independent meaning that can be classified as particular or universal. Why is 'al-‘ālam' a noun and how does it differ from the particle 'bi' (with)?

Al-Mushtarak: Words with Multiple Equal Meanings
Defines mushtarak as a word with multiple equal meanings without transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mushtarak word and distinguish it from a manqul word based on the presence or absence of transfer (naql).

Core Idea
A homonym carries multiple meanings at once, with no one meaning derived from another.

Words with multiple meanings are common in Arabic, but not all are the same. Some words, like 'ayn', carry several unrelated meanings equally, without any historical transfer. These are called mushtarak (homonyms). Understanding mushtarak is crucial for interpreting texts correctly, because the intended meaning depends on context, not on priority. In this lesson, you will learn how to spot a mushtarak and why it matters for Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its multiple meanings
Find the word in question and list all its distinct meanings as given in the source. For example, 'al-ayn' in Arabic can mean the eye, a water spring, a well, or gold.
Use a reliable dictionary if needed.
2
Check for chronological priority or transfer
Ask: Was one meaning assigned first and then the word was moved to another meaning? If not, and all meanings are assigned on an equal footing without any transfer (naql), then it is mushtarak.
The source says 'without regard to the first meaning' – no meaning is derived from another.
3
Confirm equal footing
Ensure that the word is used for each meaning in exactly the same way, with no sense of one being original or metaphorical.
If one meaning is historically prior but the other meanings are not derived from it, it may still be mushtarak if the lexical assignment was simultaneous.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the word 'qur'' in the Qur'an. It means both 'menstruation' and 'purity' in different contexts. Does it have a transfer? According to scholars, it is mushtarak because both meanings are equally established in the language without one being derived from the other.
This affects rulings in fiqh, e.g., waiting period ('idda) of a divorced woman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الألفاظ
وإن كان الثاني - أي إن كان المعنى كثيراً – فإما أن يتخلل بين تلك المعاني نقل - بأن كان موضوعاً لمعنى أولاً ثم لوحظ ذلك المعنى ووضع لمعنى آخر المناسبة بينهما - أو لم يتخلّل، فإن لم يتخلل النقل، بل كان وضعه لتلك المعاني على السوية - أي كماكان موضوعاً لهذا المعنى يكون موضوعاً لذلك المعنى من غير نظر إلى المعنى الأول) - فهو «المشترك» لاشتراكه بين تلك المعاني - كالعين، فإنّها موضوعة للباصرة والماء والركية والذهب على السواء.
وإن تخلل بين تلك المعاني نقل : فإما أن يترك استعماله في المعنى الأول، أو لا؛ فإن تُرك يسمّى «لفظاً منقولاً لنقله من المعنى الأول. والناقل إما الشرع، فيكون «منقولاً شرعيا» كالصلاة والصوم، فإنهما في الأصل للدعاء ومطلق الإمساك ثم نقلهما الشرع إلى الأركان المخصوصة والإمساك المخصوص مع النية.
وإما غير الشرع : وهو إما العرف العام، فهو «المنقــول العــربي» كـ«الدابة» فإنها في أصل اللغة لكلّ مايدبّ على الأرض، ثم نقله العرف العام إلى ذوات القوائم الأربع " من الخيل والبغال والحمير.
أو العرف الخاص : يسمى «منقولاً اصطلاحياً» كاصطلاح النحاة والنظار ؛ أما اصطلاح النحاة فكـ «الفعل»، فإنه كان اسماً لما صدر عن الفاعل - كالأكل والشرب والضرب - ثم نقله النحاة إلى «كلمة دلّت على معنى في نفسه مقترن بأحد الأزمنة الثلاثة»؛ وأما اصطلاح النظار فكـ «الدوران» فإنّه كان في الأصل للحركة في السكك ، ثم نقله النظار إلى ترتب الأثر على ماله صلوح العلية.
Topics on Words. If the second [case] – i.e., if the meaning is multiple – then either there is a transfer (naql) among those meanings – by being first assigned to one meaning, then that meaning is observed and assigned to another meaning due to an appropriateness between them – or there is no transfer. If there is no transfer, but rather its assignment to those meanings is on an equal footing – i.e., just as it is assigned to this meaning, it is assigned to that meaning without regard to the first meaning – then it is 'al-mushtarak' (homonym) because of its sharing among those meanings – like 'al-'ayn', which is assigned to the eye, water, well, and gold equally. If there is a transfer among those meanings, then either its usage in the first meaning is abandoned, or not. If abandoned, it is called 'manqul' (transferred word) due to its transfer from the first meaning. The transferrer is either the divine law (shar'), then it is 'manqul shar'i' like 'salat' and 'sawm'; originally they meant supplication and absolute abstention, then the Law transferred them to the specific pillars and specific abstention with intention. Or [the transferrer] is other than the divine law: either general custom ('urf 'amm), so it is 'al-manqul al-'urfi' like 'al-dabba'; in original language it means every creature that crawls on the earth, then general custom transferred it to quadrupeds – horses, mules, and donkeys. Or specific custom ('urf khass), it is called 'manqul istilahi' like the technical terms of grammarians and dialecticians. As for the grammarians' terminology, like 'al-fi'l' (verb); it was originally a name for what emanates from the agent – like eating, drinking, and hitting – then the grammarians transferred it to 'a word indicating a meaning in itself associated with one of the three tenses'. As for the dialecticians' terminology, like 'al-dawaran' (circularity); it was originally for movement in the streets, then the dialecticians transferred it to the ordering of an effect upon that which has the potential for causality.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion from a commentary (sharh) on the Shamsiyya. It classifies words based on how they relate to multiple meanings. The author first defines 'al-mushtarak' (homonym) as a word assigned to multiple meanings equally, without any priority or transfer. The example given is the Arabic word 'al-'ayn' which can mean the eye, a water spring, a well, or gold – all equally without one being derived from another. Then he turns to cases where there is a transfer (naql) from one meaning to another. If the original meaning ceases to be used, the word is called 'manqul' (transferred). He then divides the transferrer into three categories: (1) Shar'i: transfer by Islamic law, e.g., 'salat' originally meant 'supplication' but in the Law refers specifically to the ritual prayer with its pillars; similarly 'sawm' originally meant 'absolute abstention' but now means fasting with intention from dawn to dusk. (2) 'Urfi 'amm: transfer by general custom, e.g., 'dabba' originally meant any crawling creature but in common usage now refers specifically to quadrupeds like horses, mules, and donkeys. (3) 'Urfi khass: transfer by a specific technical community, e.g., grammarians' term 'fi'l' originally meant any action but in grammar it means a verb marked by tense; or 'dawaran' originally meant physical movement around something but in logic/dialectic it means the circular ordering of cause and effect. This classification is foundational for later discussions of figurative language and ambiguity in the Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
المشترك
النقل
على السواء

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak word carries multiple meanings simultaneously with no historical priority among them.

Al-Manqul: Words Transferred to New Meanings
Defines manqul and its three subtypes based on the transferrer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a manqul word as shar'i, urfi 'amm, or urfi khass based on the source of its transfer.

Core Idea
A transferred word abandons its original meaning for a new one through divine law, general custom, or specialised convention.

Many Arabic words started with one meaning and were later moved to a new, often narrower meaning. This process is called naql (transfer). The author distinguishes three types of transferrers: the divine law (shar'), general custom ('urf 'amm), and specialised custom ('urf khass). Recognising these categories is essential for correctly understanding classical texts, especially in fiqh, grammar, and logic. In this lesson, you will learn to identify the type of naql and apply it to real examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its original meaning
Look up the word's pre-Islamic or lexical origin. For example, 'salat' originally meant 'supplication' (du'a') in the language.
Use classical lexicons like Lisan al-Arab.
2
Identify the new meaning
Determine the meaning it carries after transfer. For 'salat', it came to mean the specific ritual prayer with pillars, conditions, and intention.
The new meaning is often more specific.
3
Determine the transferrer (naqil)
Ask: Who transferred it? If Islamic law (shar'), it is manqul shar'i. If general custom ('urf 'amm), it is manqul urfi 'amm. If a specialist community (e.g., grammarians, logicians), it is manqul urfi khass (istilahi).
The source explicitly categorises these three.
4
Check if the original meaning is abandoned
If the original meaning is no longer used in the new context (e.g., salat is never used for mere supplication in fiqh), it is manqul. If both meanings coexist, it might be mushtarak or metaphorical.
The source says 'if its usage in the first meaning is abandoned, or not' – for manqul, it is typically abandoned in the relevant sphere.
5
Classify and test with an Islamic example
Take the word 'dabba'. Original: any creature that crawls. General custom now: quadrupeds. So it is manqul urfi 'amm. For 'fi'l', grammarians transferred it from 'any action' to 'verb with tense'. That is manqul urfi khass.
This classification affects how terms are interpreted in legal arguments for the first time.

Distinguishing al-Haqiqa and al-Majaz
Author defines haqiqa and majaz with the example of 'asad' (lion).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqa and majaz in a given Islamic text and identify the relation ('alaqa) that justifies a majaz usage.

Core Idea
Haqiqa uses a word in its original meaning; majaz uses it in a transferred sense with a relation.

In Islamic legal and theological interpretation, distinguishing literal from figurative language is essential. This lesson clarifies these categories using the classic example of 'asad' (lion) and shows how the relation between original and transferred meaning determines whether a word is used literally or figuratively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word in question
Look for a word that has an original meaning and a transferred meaning. The text often signals this with examples like 'asad'.
In our passage, the example is 'al-asad'.
2
Determine the original meaning (wad')
Find what the word was originally coined for. For 'asad', the author says it was first placed for the predatory animal.
Original meaning is the first intended use of the word.
3
Determine the transferred meaning (manqul ilayh)
Identify the secondary meaning used in context. For 'asad', it is the brave man, with courage as the relation linking animal and man.
The relation must be explicit or inferable.
4
Classify usage as haqiqa or majaz
If the word is used in its original meaning, it is haqiqa. If used in the transferred meaning, it is majaz, provided the original meaning is still retained. Apply this to the example.
The passage notes that haqiqa and majaz are mutually exclusive for a given usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يترك معناه الأول بل يستعمل فيه أيضاً، يسمى «حقيقة» إن استعمل في الأول - وهو المنقول عنه - و «مجازاً» إن استعمل في الثاني وهو المنقول إليه كـ«الأسد» فإنّه وضع أوّلاً للحيوان المفترس ثم نقل إلى الرجل الشجاع العلاقة بينهما - وهي الشجاعة - فاستعماله في الأول بطريق الحقيقة وفي الثاني بطريق المجاز. أما الحقيقة فلأنها من حق فلان الأمر أي أثبته، أو من «حققته». نعم المنقول والمشترك متقابلان فلا يجتمعان وكذا الحال بين الحقيقة والمجاز (شریف).
If [the first meaning] is not abandoned but is still used in it, it is called 'haqiqa' (literal) if used in the first – which is that from which transfer occurred – and 'majaz' (figurative) if used in the second, which is that to which transfer occurred, like 'al-asad' (lion); it was first placed for the beast of prey, then transferred to the brave man due to a relationship between them – which is courage – so its use in the first is via haqiqa and in the second via majaz. As for 'haqiqa', it is from 'haqqa fulan al-'amr' (he confirmed the matter) i.e., he established it, or from 'haqqaqtuhu' (I realized it). Indeed, the transferred (manqul) and the homonym (mushtarak) are opposites and do not combine, and the same is the case between haqiqa and majaz (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of words. After discussing mushtarak (homonym) and manqul (transferred) where the original meaning is abandoned, the author now considers the case where the original meaning is retained alongside the new one. In such a case, when the word is used in its original sense, it is 'haqiqa' (literal); when used in the transferred sense, it is 'majaz' (figurative). The example is the word 'asad' (lion): originally it designated the predatory animal, but through a relation (courage) it came to be used for a brave man. Using it for the animal is haqiqa, for the brave man is majaz. The author then notes the etymology of 'haqiqa' from the verb 'haqqa' meaning to establish or confirm. Finally, he remarks that just as manqul and mushtarak are mutually exclusive, so too are haqiqa and majaz – a word can't be both haqiqa and majaz in the same sense, though it can be haqiqa in one sense and majaz in another. This distinction is crucial for understanding how Islamic scholars interpret texts, especially when dealing with legal and theological terms that may have both literal and figurative meanings.

Key Terms
الحقيقة
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the word was originally for the animal
B) Because the relation of courage exists
C) Both A and B
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
A word is haqiqa when used in its original meaning, and majaz when used in a transferred meaning via a relation, as long as the original meaning remains in use.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the Arabic word 'qalb' (قلب). Its original meaning is the physical heart as an organ. It is often used to mean 'mind' or 'center'. In the phrase 'qalb al-mas'ala' (the heart of the issue), is 'qalb' used as haqiqa or majaz? Identify the relation that connects the literal and figurative meanings.

The Definition of Compound Expression and the Criterion of Sihhat al-Sukut
Author defines compound expression and its primary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a complete compound using the criterion of sihhat al-sukut and distinguish it from an incomplete compound.

Core Idea
The compound's completeness is not about new information but about self-sufficiency in meaning.

This lesson introduces the fundamental division of all expressions into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab). Within compounds, the key distinction is between complete (tam) and incomplete (ghayr tam). The criterion is sihhat al-sukut: can the speaker stop without the listener expecting more words? We will explore this criterion and clarify a common misunderstanding about 'complete benefit'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall division
The author divides expressions into mufrad and murakkab. Locate the definition of murakkab in the Arabic.
The text begins with 'وأما المركب' after discussing mufrad.
2
Extract the criterion for completeness
Find the phrase 'صح السكوت عليه' and understand its meaning. The complete compound is that upon which it is permissible to be silent.
3
Apply the criterion to examples
Test examples like 'Zayd' vs 'Zayd is standing' to see which allows silence without expecting more.
'Zayd' alone expects a predicate; 'Zayd is standing' is complete.
4
Understand the commentator's clarification
Why does the commentator say that 'complete benefit' does not mean new information? Consider the example 'the sky is above us'.
Even known statements are complete because they are self-sufficient.
5
Distinguish complete from incomplete
Use the criterion to classify given compounds as either tam or ghayr tam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما المركب فهو إما «تام - وهو الذي يصح السكوت عليه - أو «غير تام»:
والتام إن احتمل الصدق والكذب فهو «الخبر» و«القضية» وإن لم يحتمل فهو «الإنشاء».
فإن دلّ على طلب الفعل دلالة أولية - أي وضعيّة – فهو مع الاستعلاء «أمر» كقولنا: اضرب أنت ومع الخضوع «سؤال» و «دعاء» ومع التساوي «التماس». وإن لم يدلّ فهو «تنبيه» يندرج فيه «التمني» و «الترجي» و «التعجب» و «القسم» و «النداء». وأما غير التام: فهو إما «تقييدي» كـ الحيوان الناطق» وإما «غير تقييدي كالمركب من اسم وأداة، أو كلمة وأداة.
أقول : لما فرغ من المفرد وأقسامه شرع في المركب وأقسامه، وهو إما تام أو غير تام :
لأنه إما أن يصح السكوت عليه - أي يفيد المخاطب فائدة تامة )
(۱) الأظهر أن يقال : «لأنه إما أن يفيد المخاطب فائدة تامة ، أي يصح السكوت عليه» فيجعل «صحة السكوت عليه تفسيراً للفائدة التامة، حتى لا يتوهم أن المراد بـ «الفائدة التامة» الفائدة الجديدة التي تحصل للمخاطب من المركب التام، فيلزم أن لا يكون مثل السماء فوقنا» وغيره من الأخبار المعلومة للمخاطب- مركباً تاماً، إذ لا يحصل منه للمخاطب فائدة جديدة (شريف).
ولا يكون حينئذ مستتبعاً للفظ آخر ينتظره المخاطب - كما إذا قيل : «زيد» فيبقى المخاطب منتظراً لأن يقال «قائم» أو «قاعد» مثلاً، بخلاف ما إذا قيل : «زيد قائم» - وإما أن لا يصح السكوت عليه :
فإن صح السكوت عليه فهو المركب التام» وإلا فهو «المركب الناقص» و «غير التام».
He said: As for the compound, it is either 'complete' - which is that upon which it is permissible to be silent - or 'incomplete'. The complete, if it admits truth and falsehood, is 'statement' and 'proposition'; if it does not admit, it is 'performative'. If it indicates a request for an action by a primary indication - i.e., conventional - then with superiority it is 'command', like our saying: 'strike you'; with submission it is 'question' and 'supplication'; with equality it is 'request'. If it does not indicate, it is 'notification' which includes 'wish', 'hope', 'wonder', 'oath', and 'call'. As for the incomplete: it is either 'restrictive' like 'rational animal', or 'non-restrictive' like a compound of a noun and a particle, or a word and a particle. I say: When he finished with the single word and its divisions, he began with the compound and its divisions, and it is either complete or incomplete: because it is either such that silence upon it is permissible - i.e., it benefits the addressee a complete benefit - (1) The more apparent is to say: 'because it is either that it benefits the addressee a complete benefit, i.e., it is permissible to be silent upon it' so that 'permissibility of silence upon it' is made an interpretation of 'complete benefit', so that one does not imagine that by 'complete benefit' is meant the new benefit that occurs to the addressee from the complete compound, for then such as 'the sky is above us' and other statements known to the addressee would not be complete compounds, since no new benefit occurs to the addressee from them (al-Sharif). And it is not then that it entails another word that the addressee awaits - as when it is said 'Zayd', so the addressee remains awaiting that it be said 'standing' or 'sitting', for example, unlike when it is said 'Zayd is standing' - and either that silence upon it is not permissible: for if silence upon it is permissible, then it is the 'complete compound'; otherwise it is the 'incomplete compound' and 'non-complete'.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines compound expressions (al-murakkab) and divides them into complete (tam) and incomplete (ghayr tam). The author, after discussing single words (al-mufrad), begins with the compound. The criterion for completeness is 'sihhat al-sukut' (the permissibility of being silent upon it), meaning that the utterance gives a complete benefit to the listener such that they do not expect another word to follow. The commentary clarifies that 'complete benefit' does not mean a new piece of information; otherwise, known statements like 'the sky is above us' would not be considered complete, which is incorrect. The complete compound is further divided into statements (khabar/proposition) that admit truth and falsehood, and performatives (insha') that do not. Insha' includes commands, questions, supplications, etc., based on the nature of the request. The incomplete compound is divided into restrictive (taqyidi), like 'rational animal' which restricts a noun, and non-restrictive (ghayr taqyidi), like a compound of a noun and particle. The commentary then reiterates that if the compound allows silence, it is complete; otherwise incomplete.

Key Takeaway
The criterion for a complete compound is sihhat al-sukut, not the novelty of information.

Sub-classification of Complete and Incomplete Compounds
Author subdivides complete and incomplete compounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a complete compound as khabar (proposition) or insha' (performative) and an incomplete compound as restrictive (taqyidi) or non-restrictive.

Core Idea
Complete compounds divide into statements (truth-bearing) and performatives (action-requesting); incomplete compounds restrict meaning or link words.

Having established the primary division, we now explore the finer categories. Complete compounds are either khabar, which can be true or false, or insha', which includes commands, requests, and other non-truth-bearing utterances. Incomplete compounds are either restrictive, like 'rational animal', which adds a qualification, or non-restrictive, like a noun+particle compound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sub-divisions of complete compounds
Look for the terms 'khabar' and 'insha'' and their definitions. Note that khabar admits truth/falsehood, insha' does not.
2
Analyze the types of insha' based on request and authority
Note how commands, questions, supplications are distinguished by superiority, submission, equality. Also note non-request insha' like wish, hope, wonder.
3
Examples of non-request insha'
Find examples like wish, hope, wonder, oath, call in the Arabic text.
4
Identify types of incomplete compounds
Distinguish between 'taqyidi' (restrictive) and 'ghayr taqyidi' (non-restrictive). Taqyidi modifies a noun, non-taqyidi typically links with particles.
5
Apply the classification to new examples
Given expressions, classify them correctly as khabar/insha' or taqyidi/ghayr taqyidi.

فإن دلّ على طلب الفعل دلالة أولية - أي وضعيّة – فهو مع الاستعلاء «أمر» كقولنا: اضرب أنت ومع الخضوع «سؤال» و «دعاء» ومع التساوي «التماس». وإن لم يدلّ فهو «تنبيه» يندرج فيه «التمني» و «الترجي» و «التعجب» و «القسم» و «النداء». وأما غير التام: فهو إما «تقييدي» كـ الحيوان الناطق» وإما «غير تقييدي كالمركب من اسم وأداة، أو كلمة وأداة.

Khabar and Insha': Defining Complete Compounds
Author divides complete compound into khabar and insha' with a clarifying footnote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khabar (statement) and insha' (performative) based on the criterion of admitting truth and falsehood in potentiality.

Core Idea
A statement's potential for truth and falsehood, not its actual value, defines it.

We have learned that a complete compound is one where silence is permissible. Now the author further divides the complete compound into two types: those that can be true or false (khabar) and those that cannot (insha'). But what does it mean for a statement to 'admit' truth and falsehood? This lesson clarifies the definition and introduces the key nuance of potentiality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
Locate in the Arabic passage the two types of complete compound: one that admits truth and falsehood (khabar) and one that does not (insha').
2
Understand the definition of khabar
The definition says khabar is 'that which admits truth and falsehood'. Note that the phrase uses the waw (and) potentially disjunctively, as explained later.
3
Recognize potentiality vs. actuality
The footnote explains that 'admission' here means potentiality. Every true statement has the potential for truth, every false statement the potential for falsehood. This prevents the definition from excluding actual statements.
4
Connect to completeness
The footnote also revisits the earlier term 'sihhat al-sukut', clarifying that a complete compound does not require another word by logical necessity, only by convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمركب التام إما أن يحتمل الصدق والكذب و هو «الخبر» و «القضية» أولا يحتمل، وهو «الإنشاء».
فإن قيل: الخبر إما أن يكون مطابقاً للواقع أو لا، فإن كان مطابقاً للواقع لم يحتمل الكذب، وإن لم يكن مطابقاً لم يحتمل الصدق، فلا خبر داخل في الحد.
فقد يجاب عنه بأن المراد بالواو الواصلة أو الفاصلة بمعنى أن الخبر هو الذي يحتمل الصدق والكذب، فكل خبر صادق يحتمل الصدق، وكل خبر كاذب يحتمل الكذب.
(۱) هذا تفسير أيضاً لصحة السكوت، إذ فيه نوع إبهام أيضاً، كأنه قال : المراد بصحة سكوت المتكلّم على المركب أن لا يكون ذلك المركب مستدعياً للفظ آخر استدعاء المحكوم عليه للمحكوم به أو بالعكس، فلايكون المخاطب حينئذ منتظراً للفظ آخر كانتظاره للمحكوم به عند ذكر المحكوم عليه، وانتظار المحكوم عليه عند ذكر المحكوم به ؛ وقد أشار إلى أن المراد بـ «الاستتباع» أي الاستدعاء. وبـ«الانتظار» المنفيين ماذكره بقوله : كما إذا قيل : زيد... الخ. وحينئذ لا يتجه أن يقال : يلزم أن لا يكون مثل «ضرب زيد» مركباً تاماً، لأن المخاطب منتظر إلى أن يبين المضروب ويقال «عمراً» إلى غير ذلك من القيود - كالزمان والمكان (شريف).
And the complete compound is either that which admits truth and falsehood, and it is 'statement' and 'proposition', or it does not admit, and it is 'performative'. 
For it is said: The statement is either conforming to reality or not; if it is conforming to reality, it does not admit falsehood, and if it is not conforming, it does not admit truth. So no statement falls under the definition. 
So it may be answered about it that what is meant by the connecting or separating 'waw' is that the statement is that which admits truth and falsehood. Thus every true statement admits truth, and every false statement admits falsehood. 
(1) This is also an interpretation of 'sihhat al-sukut', for there is a type of ambiguity in it as well, as if he said: The meaning of the speaker's permissible silence upon the compound is that the compound does not require another word in the way that the subject requires the predicate or vice versa. So the addressee is not then waiting for another word as he waits for the predicate when the subject is mentioned, and the waiting for the subject when the predicate is mentioned. And he indicated that by 'istitbā' meaning requirement, and by 'intizār' (waiting) which are denied, as in his saying: as when it is said 'Zayd' etc. And then it is not valid to say that 'Zayd struck' would not be a complete compound, because the addressee is waiting for the one struck to be clarified, and say 'Amr' and other restrictions such as time and place (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of complete compounds. It states that the complete compound is either 'khabar' (statement) which admits truth and falsehood, or 'insha'' (performative) which does not. Then an objection is raised: if khabar is defined as that which admits truth and falsehood, no actual statement falls under this definition because a true statement cannot be false and a false statement cannot be true. The answer clarifies that the 'waw' in the definition is disjunctive (wāṣila or fāṣila), meaning the phrase 'admits truth and falsehood' is a potentiality: every true statement admits truth, and every false statement admits falsehood. Potentiality, not actuality, is what matters. The footnote expands on the earlier concept of 'sihhat al-sukut', explaining that a compound's completeness is determined by not requiring another word as a subject requires a predicate. It addresses a potential counterexample like 'Zayd struck' where the object is missing, but argues that the addressee awaits the object only by linguistic convention, not logical necessity.

احتمال الصدق والكذب
الإنشاء
صحة السكوت

B) It must be actually false.
C) It has the potential to be true or false.
D) It is a complete compound that does not admit truth or falsehood.

Key Takeaway
Khabar is defined by its potential for truth and falsehood, not its actual truth value, which preserves the definition for all statements.

Resolving the Objection: Potentiality vs. Actuality
Author presents and resolves an objection regarding the definition of khabar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the apparent contradiction in defining khabar by explaining the disjunctive interpretation of 'waw' and the concept of potentiality.

Core Idea
The objection confuses potentiality with actuality; the answer clarifies that 'admission' is disjunctive potential.

A sharp objection is raised: if khabar is defined as that which admits truth and falsehood, then no actual statement qualifies—because a true statement cannot be false and vice versa. The author provides a concise answer by interpreting the 'waw' in the definition disjunctively. Let's analyze this logical move.

Guided Path
1
State the objection clearly
The objector argues: any khabar is either true (conforming to reality) or false (not conforming). If true, it cannot admit falsehood; if false, it cannot admit truth. Thus, no actual khabar meets the definition.
This is a valid logical argument if 'admit' is taken as actuality.
2
Identify the key term 'waw al-wasila aw al-fasila'
The answer key is the phrase 'admits truth and falsehood'. The 'waw' here is interpreted as disjunctive—meaning 'or'. So the definition says a khabar admits truth OR falsehood, not both together.
Disjunctive reading: the statement has the potential for one of the two.
3
Apply the disjunctive interpretation
Thus, every true statement admits truth (potential for truth) and every false statement admits falsehood (potential for falsehood). Each actual statement satisfies one side of the disjunction, so it falls under the definition.
4
Evaluate the objection's mistake
The objector wrongly assumed that 'admits' means the statement must simultaneously have the potential for both truth and falsehood in its current state. The correct reading is that the statement as a type has the potential for one or the other.

(1) This is also an interpretation of 'sihhat al-sukut', for there is a type of ambiguity in it as well, as if he said: The meaning of the speaker's permissible silence upon the compound is that the compound does not require another word in the way that the subject requires the predicate or vice versa. So the addressee is not then waiting for another word as he waits for the predicate when the subject is mentioned, and the waiting for the subject when the predicate is mentioned. And he indicated that by 'istitbā' meaning requirement, and by 'intizār' (waiting) which are denied, as in his saying: as when it is said 'Zayd' etc. And then it is not valid to say that 'Zayd struck' would not be a complete compound, because the addressee is waiting for the one struck to be clarified, and say 'Amr' and other restrictions such as time and place (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of complete compounds. It states that the complete compound is either 'khabar' (statement) which admits truth and falsehood, or 'insha'' (performative) which does not. Then an objection is raised: if khabar is defined as that which admits truth and falsehood, no actual statement falls under this definition because a true statement cannot be false and a false statement cannot be true. The answer clarifies that the 'waw' in the definition is disjunctive (wāṣila or fāṣila), meaning the phrase 'admits truth and falsehood' is a potentiality: every true statement admits truth, and every false statement admits falsehood. Potentiality, not actuality, is what matters. The footnote expands on the earlier concept of 'sihhat al-sukut', explaining that a compound's completeness is determined by not requiring another word as a subject requires a predicate. It addresses a potential counterexample like 'Zayd struck' where the object is missing, but argues that the addressee awaits the object only by linguistic convention, not logical necessity.

Key Terms
خبر
احتمال الصدق والكذب
واو الواصلة أو الفاصلة
مطابق للواقع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that no actual statement fits the definition of khabar?
A) By saying the definition applies only to potential statements.
B) By explaining that 'waw' in the definition is conjunctive.
C) By explaining that 'waw' is disjunctive and 'admission' means potentiality.
D) By changing the definition of khabar.

Key Takeaway
The objection to the definition of khabar is resolved by interpreting 'waw' as disjunctive, so a statement's potential for truth or falsehood defines it, not its actual truth value.

What Does Prohibition Demand? Self-Restraint or Non-Existence?
Author presents two mutakallimun views on what is demanded by prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on what prohibition (nahy) demands and evaluate the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Prohibition may demand an action (self-restraint) rather than mere non-existence.

In Usul al-Fiqh, the nature of prohibition is debated: does the command 'Do not do X' demand that X never occurs (non-existence), or does it demand that you actively refrain (self-restraint)? This lesson examines two mutakallimun positions and their implications for understanding divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
The passage presents two groups of mutakallimun. The first group argues that what is demanded by prohibition is self-restraint (kaf al-nafs), not non-existence. The second group maintains it is non-existence (adam al-fi'l).
2
Extract the first group's argument
The first group says non-existence is continuous from eternity, so it is not within human power (mamnu' ) nor acquired by the servant. Therefore, the demand cannot be for non-existence; it must be for an act—the act of self-restraint.
They assert that prohibition shares with command the fact that both demand an act.
3
Understand the implication for command
If prohibition demands self-restraint, it can be included under command (since command is a demand for an act). Some scholars exclude it by restricting command to 'an act that is not self-restraint'.
This highlights the conceptual overlap between command and prohibition.
4
Extract the second group's argument
The second group responds: non-existence is within power because one can choose to perform the act (ending non-existence) or refrain (continuing it). Thus, the demand for non-existence is meaningful and power-related.
This view treats non-existence as something the servant can preserve or terminate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهبت جماعة من المتكلمين إلى أن المطلوب بالنهي ليس هو عدم الفعل - كما هو المتبادر إلى الفهم - لأن عدمه مستمر من الأزل إلى الأبد، فلايكون مقدوراً للعبد ولا حاصلاً بتحصيله، بل المطلوب به هو كف النفس عن الفعل وحينئذ يشارك النهي الأمر في أن المطلوب بهما هو الفعل، إلا أن المطلوب بالنهي فعل مخصوص هو الكف عن فعل آخر ، و حينئذ يمكن إدراجه في الأمر كما ذكره، ويمكن إخراجه عنه بأن يقيد الأمر بأنه طلب فعل غير كف، كما فعله بعضهم. وذهب جماعة أخرى منهم إلى أن المطلوب بالنهي هو عدم الفعل، وهو مقدور للعبد باعتبار استمراره، إذ له أن يفعل الفعل فيزول استمرار عدمه، وله أن لا يفعله فيستمر (شريف).
A group of mutakallimun held that what is demanded by prohibition is not the non-existence of the act—as is immediately understood—because its non-existence is continuous from eternity to eternity, so it is not within the power of the servant nor attained by his acquisition. Rather, what is demanded by it is the restraint of the self from the act. Then prohibition shares with command in that what is demanded by both is an act, except that what is demanded by prohibition is a specific act, which is restraint from another act. And then it can be included under command as mentioned, and it can be excluded from it by restricting command to requesting an act that is not restraint, as some have done. Another group among them held that what is demanded by prohibition is the non-existence of the act, and it is within the power of the servant with respect to its continuity, because he can do the act and then the continuity of its non-existence ceases, and he can not do it and thus it continues (Sharif).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a critical discussion about the nature of prohibition (nahy). The author first reports the view of a group of mutakallimun who argue that what is demanded by a prohibition is not the non-existence of the act (since non-existence is eternal and not within human power), but rather the act of self-restraint (kaf al-nafs). This would make prohibition similar to command in that both demand an act—the difference being that the commanded act is positive while the prohibited act is a restraint. They note that this allows restriction of command to acts that are not restraint, as some scholars did. Then the author presents a second group who maintain that what is demanded is indeed non-existence, but this is still within human power because one can choose to commit the act (ending the non-existence) or refrain (preserving it). The marginal note (Sharif) indicates this is a subject of debate.

C) Because the act of self-restraint is easier to perform
D) Because non-existence cannot be described as a demand

Key Takeaway
Prohibition (nahy) is debated as either demanding an act of self-restraint or demanding the continuation of non-existence, with each position having implications for the relationship between command and prohibition in Usul al-Fiqh.

The Definition of Particular and Universal Concepts
Author defines simple concepts and divides them into particular and universal by shareability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a concept is particular (جزئي) or universal (كلي) by applying the criterion of prevention of sharing in its very conception.

Core Idea
A concept is universal if its conception allows sharing, particular if it prevents.

In this passage, the author lays the groundwork for all subsequent logical discussions by defining the fundamental distinction between particular and universal concepts. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly analyzing definitions and predications in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh and kalam. The commentary clarifies that the criterion is internal to the concept itself.

Guided Path
1
Locate the core definition
Find the sentence that defines 'particular' and 'universal'. The key phrase is 'إن منع نفس تصوّره من وقوع الشركة فيه' for particular, and 'إن لم يمنع' for universal.
2
Extract the criterion for particularity
Particularity is defined by prevention of sharing by the very conception. Note that the prevention is not from external factors but from the concept itself as conceived.
3
Extract the criterion for universality
Universality is defined by the absence of such prevention. The concept does not block the mind from supposing it applies to many.
4
Apply the criterion to the text examples
Test 'this human' (هذا الإنسان): its conception prevents sharing, so it is particular. Test 'human' (الإنسان): its conception allows sharing, so it is universal.
5
Test your understanding with a new Islamic example
Consider 'the Kaaba' (الكعبة) – is its conception shareable? No, it refers to a specific building, so particular. Consider 'mosque' (مسجد) – yes, shareable, so universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثاني في المعاني المفردة. كل مفهوم فهو «جزئيّ» إن منع نفس تصوّره من وقوع الشركة فيه و «كلّيّ» إن لم يمنع واللفظ الدال عليهما يسمّى «كلياً» و «جزئيّاً» بالعرض. أقول : المعاني هي الصور الذهنية من حيث أنها وضع بإزائها الألفاظ، فإن عبر عنها بألفاظ مفردة فهي المعاني المفردة والا فالمركبة؛ والكلام هاهنا إنما هو في المعاني المفردة كما ستعرف. فكل مفهوم - وهو الحاصل في العقل - إما جزئي أو كلي، لأنه إما أن يكون نفس تصوّره - أي من حيث أنه متصور - مانعاً من وقوع الشركة فيه - أي من اشتراكه بين كثيرين وصدقه عليها - أو لا يكون، فإن منع نفس تصوّره عن الشركة فهو الجزئي كهذا الإنسان، فإنّ الهذية [كذا، ولعل الصواب: الذهنية] إذا حصل مفهومها عند العقل امتنع العقل بمجرد تصوره عن صدقه على متعدد. وإن لم يمنع الشركة من حيث أنه متصور فهو «الكلي» كالإنسان. ملخص الكلام أن ما حصل في العقل فهو بمجرد حصوله فيه إن امتنع في العقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين فهو الجزئي، كذات زيد، فإنه إذا حصل عند العقل استحال أن يفرض صدقه على كثيرين، وإلا - أي وإن لم يمتنع بمجرد حصوله فيه فرض صدقه على كثيرين - فهو الكلي، فالكلية إمكان فرض الاشتراك، والجزئية استحالته. لما كان ظاهر العبارة يدل على أن المانع من الشركة هو نفس تصوره نبه على أن المراد منع ذلك المفهوم من حيث أنه متصور.
He said: The second chapter on simple concepts. Every concept is 'particular' if its very conception prevents occurrence of sharing in it, and 'universal' if it does not prevent; the word indicating them is called 'universal' and 'particular' accidentally. I say: The meanings are the mental forms inasmuch as words are placed to indicate them; if they are expressed by single words then they are simple meanings, otherwise compound; and the discourse here is only about simple meanings as you will know. So every concept – which is what is acquired in the mind – is either particular or universal, because either its very conception – i.e. inasmuch as it is conceived – prevents occurrence of sharing in it – i.e. its being shared among many and being true of them – or it does not. If its very conception prevents sharing, then it is the particular, like 'this human'; for the mental [form] when its concept is acquired in the mind, the mind, merely by its conception, is prevented from its being true of many. And if it does not prevent sharing inasmuch as it is conceived, then it is the universal, like 'human'. Summary of the discourse: What is acquired in the mind – if by its mere acquisition in it the mind is prevented from supposing it to be true of many, then it is the particular, like the essence of Zayd; for when it is acquired in the mind it is impossible to suppose it true of many; otherwise – i.e. if by its mere acquisition in it the supposition of its being true of many is not prevented – then it is the universal. So universality is the possibility of supposing sharing, and particularity is its impossibility. Since the apparent wording indicates that the preventer of sharing is its very conception, he pointed out that what is meant is the prevention of that concept inasmuch as it is conceived.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a definitional lesson from the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. It opens the second chapter on simple concepts (المعاني المفردة). The author first provides the core definition: every concept (مفهوم) is either particular (جزئي) if its mere conception prevents sharing among many individuals, or universal (كلي) if it does not. The word that signifies such a concept is called universal or particular in a derivative sense. The commentator then explains that 'meanings' (المعاني) are mental forms considered as objects of linguistic convention; when expressed by a single word they are simple, otherwise compound. The focus here is on simple concepts. He reiterates that every mental content (المفهوم) is either particular or universal based on whether its conception alone prevents multiple instantiation. For example, the concept of 'this human' (هذا الإنسان) is particular because the mind cannot conceive it as applying to more than one individual; whereas 'human' (الإنسان) is universal because it can be predicated of many. The commentator summarizes: if the mind, upon acquiring a concept, cannot suppose it to be true of many, it is particular; if it can, it is universal. Thus universality is the possibility of supposing sharing, particularity its impossibility. A subtle point is clarified: the prevention is from the very conception of the concept, not from an external factor. This definition of particular and universal is foundational for the subsequent discussion of the five universals in logic.

Key Terms
مفهوم (mafhūm): What is acquired in the mind, i.e., a mental content or concept. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
جزئي (juz'ī): A concept whose very conception prevents it from being shared among many (i.e., prevents multiple instantiation). [Note: In later texts, juz'ī often means 'particular' as opposed to 'universal', but the definition via prevention of sharing is standard in Mantiq.]
كلي (kullī): A concept whose conception does not prevent sharing among many; i.e., it is predicable of more than one. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
معنى مفرد (maʿnā mufrad): A meaning expressed by a single word, as opposed to compound (مركب) which is expressed by a composite expression. [Note: In some later logic texts, 'mufrad' may also refer to incomposite concepts regardless of expression; here it is tied to the word.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in the passage, which of the following is a 'particular' (جزئي) concept?
A) الإنسان (al-insān, 'the human')
B) هذا الإنسان (hādhā al-insān, 'this human')
C) الحيوان (al-ḥayawān, 'the animal')
D) الجسم (al-jism, 'the body')

Key Takeaway
The essential logical point: a concept's particularity or universality is determined solely by whether its very conception allows or prevents sharing among many.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the concept 'القرآن' (al-Qur'an) is particular or universal according to the criterion. Explain your reasoning.
?
Provide an example from Islamic knowledge where a concept is universal, and explain why it is not particular.

Critique of the Definition of Species and Proposed Reformulation
Critique of species definition and reformulation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify redundancy and coverage failure in a definition of a five universal and propose a refined definition that avoids both problems.

Core Idea
A good definition must neither be redundant nor exclude valid instances – the definition of species fails both.

The standard definition of species as 'said of many and said of one' is scrutinized here. The author exposes a dilemma: either the phrase 'said of one' is superfluous, or the definition fails to cover species with no external individuals. This lesson walks through the critique and the proposed fix, clarifying the crucial distinction between actual and potential predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique
Locate the original definition of species: 'said of many and said of one' (from earlier in the text). Recognize that 'many' is ambiguous.
2
Determine the two possible meanings of 'many'
Consider two interpretations: (a) 'many' refers to any multitude, including mental existence; (b) 'many' refers only to externally existing individuals.
This is the key to the dilemma.
3
Test each meaning for redundancy and comprehensiveness
If 'many' means any multitude, then 'said of one' becomes redundant because a species with few external individuals is already said of many (in the mind). If 'many' means external existence, then species like the phoenix (no external individuals) are excluded, making the definition non-comprehensive.
The phoenix (عنقاء) is the classic example of a species with no existing individuals.
4
Recognize the reformulation
The author proposes deleting 'said of one' and even the word 'universal', because 'said of many' (interpreted as potential) alone suffices. The new definition: 'Species is that which is said of many agreeing in reality in answer to what is it.'
This definition applies to every species, whether it has actual individuals or not.
5
Understand the defense against the actuality objection
An objector might say 'universal' means 'fit to be said' (potential), while 'said of many' might mean 'actually said'. The author responds that in definitions of universals, 'said of many' is intended to mean potential, not actual. If it meant actual, then universals without instances would be excluded, which is unacceptable.
Implicative indication (الالتزام) is not considered in definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهناك نظر، و هو أن أحد الأمرين لازم، إما اشتمال التعريف على أمر مستدرج، وإما أن لا يكون التعريف جامعاً : لأن المراد بـ «الكثيرين» إن كان مطلقاً - سواء كانوا موجودين في الخارج أو لم يكونوا - فيلزم أن يكون قوله المقول على واحد زائداً حشواً، لأن النوع الغير المتعدّد الأشخاص في الخارج مقول على كثيرين موجودين في الذهن، وإن كان المراد بـ «الكثيرين» الموجودين في الخارج : يخرج عن التعريف الأنواع التي لا وجود لها في الخارج أصلاً - كالعنقاء - فلايكون جامعاً.
والصواب أن يحذف من التعريف قوله «على واحد»، بل لفظ «الكلّي» أيضاً ، فإنّ «المقول على كثيرين» يغني عنه، ويقال «النوع هو المقول على كثيرين متفقين بالحقيقة في جواب ما هو »، وحينئذ يكون كل نوع مقولاً في جواب «ما هو ؟» بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معاً.
(۱) وذلك أن مفهوم «الكلي» هو مفهوم المقول على كثيرين بعينه، إلا أن لفظ «الكلي» يدل عليه إجمالا، ولفظ «المقول على كثيرين» تفصيلاً. لا يقال : مفهوم الكلّي هو الصالح لأن يقال بالفرض على كثيرين، ومفهوم المقول على كثيرين ما كان مقولاً على كثيرين بالفعل، فلايغني عنه، لأن دلالة «المقول بالفعل» على الصالح لأن يقال على كثيرين» التزام، ودلالة الالتزام ليست معتبرة في التعريفات. لأنا نقول : لم يرد بـ «المقول على كثيرين في تعريف الكليات إلا الصالح لأن يقال على كثيرين، إذ لو أريد به المقول بالفعل» لخرج عن تعريف الكليات مفهومات كلية ليس لها أفراد موجودة في الخارج ولا في الذهن، فإنّها لا تكون مقولة بالفعل بل بالصلاحية، فيكون المقول على كثيرين بمعنى الكلي، فيغني عنه (شريف).
And there is a criticism, which is that one of two things is necessary: either the definition includes something redundant, or it is not comprehensive. Because if by 'many' is meant absolutely – whether they exist externally or not – then his statement 'said of one' becomes superfluous, since a species with few individuals externally is said of many existing in the mind. And if by 'many' is meant those existing externally, then the definition excludes species that have no external existence at all – like the phoenix – so it is not comprehensive. The correct thing is to delete from the definition his statement 'on one', rather also the word 'universal', for 'said of many' suffices for it, and it is said: 'Species is that which is said of many agreeing in reality in answer to what is it'. Then every species is said in answer to 'what is it' both by way of commonality and particularity. (1) That is because the concept of 'universal' is exactly the concept of 'said of many', except that the word 'universal' indicates it summarily, and the word 'said of many' in detail. It might be said: The concept of universal is that which is fit to be said by hypothesis of many, while the concept of 'said of many' is that which is actually said of many, so it does not suffice for it, because the indication of 'actually said' to 'fit to be said of many' is implicative, and implicative indication is not considered in definitions. But we say: By 'said of many' in the definition of universals is meant only that which is fit to be said of many, for if 'actually said' were meant, then the definition would exclude universal concepts that have no individuals existing externally or in the mind, for they are not actually said but only by fitness, so 'said of many' is in the sense of universal, and thus suffices for it (Sharif).
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents a critical refinement of the definition of species given earlier. The author identifies a dilemma in the standard definition (species is said of many... said of one, etc.): either the phrase 'said of one' is redundant or the definition is not comprehensive. If 'many' means any many (including mental), then 'said of one' is superfluous. If 'many' means existing externally, then species like the phoenix (which has no external individuals) are excluded, making the definition not comprehensive. The proposed solution is to remove 'said of one' and even the word 'universal' (kulli) because 'said of many' already implies universality. The new definition: 'Species is that which is said of many agreeing in reality in answer to what is it'. This ensures every species is predicated in answer to 'what is it' both by sharing (with other species) and by particularity (for its individuals). The text then addresses an objection: 'universal' means 'fit to be said of many' (by hypothesis), while 'said of many' could mean 'actually said'. The response clarifies that in definitions of universals, 'said of many' means 'fit to be said of many' (potential), not actual. If it meant actual, then concepts without any individuals (like phoenix) would not be universal. Thus 'said of many' suffices and is equivalent to 'universal'. This passage demonstrates a typical logical critique: analyzing definitions for redundancy and comprehensiveness, and refining them to be precise. The example of the phoenix (anqa') is used to illustrate a species with no external existence.

Key Terms
الكلي
المقول على كثيرين
الصلاحية
الالتزام
النوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because 'said of many' already implies universality.
B) Because species is not always universal.
C) Because 'universal' is not a proper logical term.
D) Because the definition should avoid all synonyms.

Key Takeaway
The definition of species must avoid both redundant parts and exclusion of valid instances; 'said of many' suffices if interpreted as potential predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Counting Definitions in the Genus Tree
Author explains rule for counting definitions in genus hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of definitions required for any species given its genus hierarchy.

Core Idea
Number of definitions = degrees of genus separation + 1.

In this lesson, we learn a straightforward rule from the logic of definitions: the number of complete definitions (hudud) for a species equals the number of its ascending genera plus one. This rule helps us systematically enumerate all the definitions of a thing, from the most specific (proximate genus + differentia) to the most general. Understanding this ensures we do not miss any legitimate definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the species and its proximate genus
Determine the species (e.g., 'insan') and the immediate genus directly above it (e.g., 'hayawan').
2
Ascend the genus hierarchy
List all higher genera up to the highest relevant category (e.g., 'jism', 'jawhar').
3
Count degrees of distance
Count how many genera are above the species (excluding the species itself). For insan with genera hayawan and jism, degrees = 2.
4
Apply the rule
Number of definitions = degrees + 1. So for insan, definitions = 3.
5
Enumerate the definitions
The first definition uses the proximate genus (hayawan natiq), the second uses the next genus (jism hasas), the third uses the highest genus (jism).
Each definition uses a different genus and appropriate differentia.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كرم الرسالة الشمسية والجسم النامي والجسم المطلق أجوبة ثلاثة وهو جواب رابع ؛ وعلى هذا القياس ؛ فكلما يزيد البعد يزيد عدد الأجوبة ويكون عدد الأجوبة زائداً على عدد مراتب البعد بواحد، لأنّ الجنس القريب جواب، ولكل مرتبة من مراتب البعد جواب آخر.

Commentary
This passage explains a rule about the number of definitions (hudud) required as we ascend the hierarchy of genera. The author uses an example: for a species, there are definitions for each genus in its chain. The 'growing body' (al-jism al-nami) and 'absolute body' (al-jism al-mutlaq) are two genera; they, along with something else, produce three answers, and the fourth answer corresponds to the species itself. The rule is: the number of answers (definitions) is one more than the number of degrees of separation from the ultimate genus. For instance, for man, we have definitions as 'rational animal' (proximate genus + differentia), then as 'sentient body', then as 'body', etc. The proximate genus provides one definition, and each higher genus adds another definition. This is crucial for understanding how tarkib al-hudud (composition of definitions) works.

Key Terms
الجنس القريب (al-jins al-qarīb): the proximate genus
البعد (al-bu'd): degree of separation
جواب (jawāb): answer, i.e., a definition

D) One definition

Key Takeaway
The number of definitions for a species equals the number of ascending genera plus one.

Proof that a Part of the Common Complete is a Differentia
Proves that a part of the complete common term is a differentia, rejecting infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that a part of the common complete must be a differentia and identify the commentator's objection about infinite regress.

Core Idea
A part of the common complete must be a differentia; otherwise infinite regress destroys the essence's unity.

This passage from the commentary on the Shamsiyya presents a rigorous proof that any part of the 'common complete' (tamam al-mushtarak) between an essence (mahiyya) and another species must ultimately serve as a differentia (fasl). The argument uses a reductio against infinite regress, showing that if the part were more general, it would lead to an impossibility. The commentator then refines the notion of infinite regress, ensuring the proof is sound. Understanding this argument is crucial for mastering the theory of definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hypothesis
Assume that some part of the common complete (between an essence and a second species) is more general (a'amm) than the common complete itself. The passage explores the consequences of this assumption.
The common complete is the full shared attribute; a part of it is less comprehensive.
2
Trace the regress
If that part is more general, it must exist in another species without the second common complete, generating a third common complete. This leads to an infinite chain of common completes unless the process stops.
The text says: 'so a third common complete obtains, and so on.'
3
Reject the infinite regress
An infinite regress of common completes is impossible because it would imply the essence is composed of infinitely many parts, which is absurd. Therefore, the regress must terminate at some part equal to the common complete.
The commentator clarifies that the regress is not of the essence's parts, but of common completes; however, the impossibility stands.
4
Determine the remaining possibility
Since the part cannot be more general (leads to regress) nor less general (trivially not a candidate), it must be equal (musawi) to the common complete. This is the only viable option.
The passage states: 'when the three divisions are false, it is determined that some part of the common complete is equal to it.'
5
Conclude that the part is a differentia
In both cases (part not common at all, or part equal to common complete), the part distinguishes the essence from others. If not common, it is specific to the essence. If equal, it distinguishes the genus (the common complete) from other genera, thereby also distinguishing the essence. Thus it is a differentia.
The definition of differentia: 'the distinguisher of the essence in general' — from partners in genus or existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحينئذ لو كان بعض تمام المشترك بين الماهية والنوع الثاني أعم منه لكان موجوداً في نوع آخر بدون تمام المشترك الثاني، فيكون مشتركاً بين الماهية وذلك النوع الثالث الذي هو بإزاء تمام المشترك الثاني - وليس تمام المشترك بينهما بل بعضه - فيحصل تمام مشترك ثالث، وهلم جراً، فإما أن يوجد تمام المشتركات إلى غير النهاية أو ينتهي إلى بعض تمام مشترك مساو له ؛ والأول محال ، وإلا لتركبت الماهية من أجزاء غير متناهية.
فقوله : «ولا يتسلسل» ليس على ما ينبغي، لأن التسلسل هو ترتب أمور غير متناهية، ولم يلزم من الدليل ترتب أجزاء الماهية، وإنما يلزم أن لو كان تمام المشترك الثاني جزء من تمام المشترك الأول، وهو غير لازم - ولعله أراد بالتسلسل وجود أمور غير متناهية في الماهية، لكنه خلاف المتعارف.
وإذا بطلت الأقسام الثلاثة تعين أن يكون بعض تمام المشترك مساوياً له وهو الأمر الثاني.
وأما أن الجزء فصل على تقدير كل واحد من الأمرين فلأنه إن لم يكن مشتركاً أصلاً يكون مختصاً بها، فيكون مميزاً للماهية عن غيرها، وإن كان بعض تمام المشترك مساوياً له يكون فصلا لتمام المشترك لاختصاصه به وتمام المشترك جنس فيكون فصلاً للماهية، لأنه لما ميز الجنس عن أغياره - وجميع أغيار الجنس بعض أغيار الماهية - فيكون مميزاً للماهية عن بعض أغيارها، ولا نعني بالفصل إلا مميز الماهية في الجملة، وإلى هذا أشار بقوله : وكيف ما كان» أي سواء لم يكن الجزء مشتركاً أصلاً أو يكون بعضاً من تمام المشترك مساوياً له، فهو مميز للماهية عن مشاركيها في جنس لها أو وجود، فيكون فصلاً.
Then, if some part of the common complete between the essence and a second species were more general than it, it would exist in another species without the second common complete, so it would be common between the essence and that third species which is opposite the second common complete—and it is not the common complete between them but rather some part of it—so a third common complete obtains, and so on. So either the common complete terms exist ad infinitum or they terminate at some part of the common complete equal to it; the first is impossible, for then the essence would be composed of infinite parts.
So his statement 'and it does not go on infinitely' is not as it should be, because infinite regress is the arrangement of infinite things, and from the proof there does not follow an arrangement of parts of the essence, but rather it would follow only if the second common complete were a part of the first common complete, which is not necessary—and perhaps he meant by infinite regress the existence of infinite things in the essence, but that is contrary to what is customary.
And when the three divisions are false, it is determined that some part of the common complete is equal to it, which is the second case.
As for the part being a differentia under each of the two cases: that is because if it is not common at all, it is specific to it, so it distinguishes the essence from others; and if it is some part of the common complete equal to it, it becomes a differentia for the common complete due to its specificity to it, and the common complete is a genus, so it becomes a differentia for the essence, because when it distinguishes the genus from its others—and all the others of the genus are some of the others of the essence—it becomes a distinguisher of the essence from some of its others. By 'differentia' we mean only the distinguisher of the essence in general. And to this he pointed by his saying 'however it may be', i.e., whether the part is not common at all or is some part of the common complete equal to it, it is a distinguisher of the essence from its partners in a genus or existence, so it becomes a differentia.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an expanded discussion from the commentary on the Shamsiyya. It provides a logical proof that a part of the 'common complete' (tamam al-mushtarak) must ultimately be a differentia (fasl). The common complete is the shared attribute between an essence (mahiyya) and another species. The argument proceeds as follows: Starting from the hypothesis that some part of the common complete is more general than it, the author shows that this leads to either an infinite regress of common completes or termination at a part equal to the common complete. Infinite regress is impossible because it would imply composition of the essence from infinite parts. The commentator then critiques the original text's use of 'tasalsul' (infinite regress), noting that the true infinite regress here would be a chain of parts of the essence, which does not necessarily follow from the proof; the original author may have meant something else. With the rejection of the three possibilities (the part being more general, leading to infinite regress, or being less general), the only remaining possibility is that the part is equal to the common complete. The commentator then explains that in both scenarios—when the part is not common at all or when it is equal to the common complete—that part serves as a differentia. In the first case, it is specific to the essence; in the second, it distinguishes the genus (the common complete) from other genera, and since distinguishing the genus is a way of distinguishing the essence, it functions as a differentia. The term 'differentia' (fasl) is defined as 'the distinguisher of the essence in general'. The passage concludes by clarifying that this distinguishing can be from partners in a genus (if the essence has a genus) or from existential partners (if it has no genus), making the part a differentia in either case.

A part of the common complete must be a differentia because any alternative leads to impossible infinite regress or fails to distinguish the essence, and the differentia is the distinguisher of the essence in general.

Definition and Classification of Differentia into Near and Far
Author defines differentia and divides it into near and far with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given differentia as near or far and explain why based on the genus level.

Core Idea
The differentia's nearness or farness depends on the genus level it distinguishes.

In this passage, the author defines the logical concept of differentia (الفصل) and introduces a key classification: near and far. Understanding this distinction is essential for constructing precise definitions in Islamic logic, which in turn underpins rigorous reasoning in Kalam, Fiqh, and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the differentia
Find the term being defined as differentia in the passage. The author defines it generally before classifying.
2
Distinguish generic vs existential partners
Note that the differentia can distinguish from either a partner in the same genus (المشارك الجنسي) or a partner in existence (المشارك الوجودي). The classification into near and far applies only to the first type.
3
Apply the near/far rule
If the differentia distinguishes from partners in the immediate (proximate) genus, it is near. If it distinguishes from partners in a remote (higher) genus, it is far.
For example, 'rational' for man distinguishes from partners in the near genus 'animal', while 'sensitive' distinguishes from partners in the far genus 'growing body'.
4
Practice with examples
Use the examples given: 'rational' is near, 'sensitive' is far. Try to classify other potential differentiae for different species following the same logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وإن ميزه عن مشاركاته في الجنس البعيد فهو «فصل بعيد» كالحساس للإنسان، فإنّه يميّزه عن مشاركاته في الجسم النامي.
وإنما اعتبر القرب والبعد في الفصل المميز للجنس، لأن الفصل

And if it distinguishes from its partners in the far genus, then it is a 'far differentia' like 'sensitive' for man, for it distinguishes him from his partners in the growing body.
