Commentary
This passage defines the differentia (فصل) and classifies it. The author begins with a brief statement about the five universals, then introduces the differentia in relation to genus. He explains that the differentia either distinguishes from a partner in the same immediate genus (المشارك الجنسي) or from a partner in existence (المشارك الوجودي). For the first type, it is further divided into near (قريب) and far (بعيد). A near differentia distinguishes the species from others in the same proximate genus, like rational (ناطق) for man differentiating him from other animals. A far differentia distinguishes from partners in a more remote genus, like sensitive (حساس) for man differentiating him from plants (which share the genus of growing body). The author then notes that the consideration of near and far is specifically for the differentia that distinguishes from the generic partner. The phrase 'لأن الفصل' is left incomplete, likely leading into the objection that follows in the next part.

The Objection: Why Division into Near/Far Differentia Seems Impossible for Existential Partners
Objector's argument against extending near/far division to existential partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objector's argument that equal components make near/far distinction impossible and identify the logical fallacy involved.

Core Idea
If components are equal, calling one near and another far is arbitrary—no rational basis.

The objector challenges the earlier restriction of near/far differentiae to generic partners, arguing that logic's rules are universal. He claims that if a quiddity is composed of equal elements, each distinguishes equally, so labeling some as near and others as far is arbitrary—a classic case of preponderance without a preponderant factor (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). This objection forces us to examine the foundations of definition and classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that division into near and far differentiae cannot be applied to existential partners (mushārakāt wujūdiyya). Find the exact statement in the Arabic.
The claim is at the beginning of the objection.
2
Extract the premise about equal components
If a quiddity (māhiyya) is composed of equal things (umūr mutasāwiya), each component distinguishes the quiddity equally. So it's impossible to call one near and one far.
The equality implies equal distinguishing power.
3
Identify the logical fallacy
Recognize that the objector invokes the principle of preponderance without a preponderant factor (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). If two equal options are distinguished arbitrarily, it's a logical fallacy.
This is a key principle in Kalam and logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعترض عليه بأن قواعد الفن عامة شاملة لجميع المفهومات سواء كانت محققة الوجود في الخارج أو لا، فلايكون تحقق الوجود مقتضياً لتخصيص البحث به، فالصواب أن يقال : اعتبار الانقسام إلى القريب والبعيد لا يتصور في الفصول المميزة عن المشاركات الوجودية، فإنّ الماهية إذا تركبت من أمور متساوية كان تمييز كل واحد منها للماهية كتمييز الآخر لها، فلا يمكن عد بعضها مميزاً قريباً وبعضها مميزاً بعيداً ، وإلا يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجّح، فلذلك خص اعتبار الانقسام إلى القريب والبعيد بالفصول المميزة عن المشاركات الجنسية. ويرد عليه أن الانقسام إليهما يتصوّر في تلك الفصول أيضاً، فإنا إذا فرضنا ماهية مركبة من جنس وفصل، وفرضنا ذلك الجنس مركبا من أمرين متساويين، كان كلّ واحد من الأمرين المتساويين فصلاً مميزاً لذلك الجنس عن المشاركات الوجودية ومميّزاً لتلك الماهية عن بعض المشاركات الوجودية فقد وجد أحوال الفصول المميزة عن المشاركات الوجودية مختلفة في التميز، فحينئذ يمكن أن يقال : الفصل المميز للماهية عما يشاركها في الوجود إن ميزها عن جميع المشاركات، فهو فصل قريب لها، وإن ميزها عن بعضها فهو فصل بعيد لها.
شرح الرسالة الشمسية
المميز في الوجود ليس متحقق الوجود، بل هو مبني على احتمال يذكر. وربما يمكن أن يستدل على بطلانه بأن يقال لو تركبت ماهية حقيقة من أمرين متساويين فإما أن لا يحتاج أحدهما إلى الآخر – وهـو محـال : ضرورة وجوب احتياج بعض أجزاء الماهية الحقيقية إلى البعض - أو يحتاج، فإن احتاج كل منهما إلى الآخر يلزم الدور، وإلا يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجّح لأنهما ذاتيان متساويان، فاحتياج أحدهما إلى الآخر ليس أولى من احتياج الآخر إليه.
أو يقال : لوتركب جنس عال - كالجوهر مثلاً - من أمرين متساويين فأحدهما إن كان عرضاً فيلزم تقوم الجوهر بالعرض – وهو محال – وإن كان جوهراً : فإما أن يكون الجوهر نفسه، فيلزم أن يكون الكل نفس جزئه - وأنه محال - أو داخلاً فيه - وهو أيضاً محال لامتناع تركب الشيء من نفسه ومن غيره - أو خارجاً عنه، فيكون عارضاً له؛ لكن ذلك الجزء ليس عارضاً لنفسه، بل يكون العارض بالحقيقة هو الجزء الآخر ، فلا يكون العارض بتمامه عارضاً - وأنه محال – فلينظر في هذا المقام فإنه من مطارح الأذكياء.
فالأولى الاقتصار على ماذكره الشارح، فإن تحقق الوجود يقتضي زيادة الاعتناء به، فربما يقتصر في بعض المباحث على ماذكره، ويحال معرفة ماعداه على المقايسة به وأما التعريفات فالأولى بها شمولها للكل (شريف).
An objection was raised against it: that the rules of the art are general, encompassing all concepts whether they have realized existence externally or not, so the realization of existence does not necessitate restricting the inquiry to it. Rather, the correct thing to say is: the consideration of division into near and far is not conceivable in the differentiae that distinguish from existential partners. For if a quiddity is composed of equal things, the distinction of each one of them for the quiddity is like the distinction of the other for it, so it is impossible to count some as near differentia and some as far differentia; otherwise circularity without a preponderant factor would follow. Therefore, the consideration of division into near and far was restricted to the differentiae distinguishing from generic partners. It is replied to him: that the division into them is conceivable in those differentia as well. For if we suppose a quiddity composed of a genus and a differentia, and suppose that genus composed of two equal things, then each of the two equal things would be a differentia distinguishing that genus from existential partners and distinguishing that quiddity from some existential partners, so the states of the differentiae distinguishing from existential partners would be found different in distinguishing. Then it can be said: the differentia that distinguishes the quiddity from what shares existence with it, if it distinguishes it from all partners, then it is a near differentia for it, and if it distinguishes it from some partners, then it is a far differentia for it. Commentary on the Shamsiyyah: The differentia distinguishing in existence is not real existence; rather, it is based on a possible mention. And perhaps it can be argued for its invalidity by saying: if a real quiddity were composed of two equal things, then either one does not need the other – and that is impossible due to the necessity of some parts of the real quiddity needing others – or it needs. If each needs the other, a circle follows; otherwise, preponderance without a preponderant factor follows because they are essential and equal, so one's need of the other is not more appropriate than the other's need of it. Or it is said: if a high genus, like substance, were composed of two equal things, then if one of them is an accident, the subsistence of substance by accident would follow – which is impossible. And if it is a substance, then either it is the substance itself, so the whole would be the same as its part – which is impossible – or it is included in it, which is also impossible due to the impossibility of composition of a thing from itself and another – or external to it, so it would be an accident to it; but that part is not an accident to itself, rather the accident in reality is the other part, so the accident would not be an accident in its entirety – and that is impossible. So consider this position, for it is among the intellectual challenges. The best is to restrict to what the commentator mentioned, for the realization of existence necessitates increased attention to it. Perhaps in some investigations one may restrict to what he mentioned, and reference knowledge of what is beyond to analogy. As for definitions, the best is that they be universal. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. It begins with an objection against the earlier division restricting near/far differentiae to those distinguishing from generic partners. The objector argues that the rules of logic are general, so the division should apply to all concepts regardless of external existence. He claims that if a quiddity is composed of equal things (أمور متساوية), the distinguishing power of each is equal, so you cannot call some near and some far; thus the division only makes sense for generic partners. The author then responds (يرد عليه) by constructing a scenario: suppose a quiddity has a genus and differentia, and that genus itself is composed of two equal things. Each of those equal things can serve as a differentia for the genus against existential partners, and also distinguish the quiddity from some existential partners. Then one can be near (if it distinguishes from all partners) and the other far (if only some). This shows the division is conceivable. The passage then includes a marginal note from 'Sharif' arguing that it's better to stick with the commentator's original restriction, because realized existence warrants extra attention, and other cases can be handled by analogy. Additionally, a proof is given against the possibility of a real quiddity composed of two equal things, using arguments of circularity and preponderance, and another proof using the example of substance and accident. The passage ends with a recommendation.

الترجيح بلا مرجّح
الماهية

B) Preponderance without a preponderant factor (al-tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ)

The Response: How Division is Conceivable for Existential Partners
Author's response to the objection, showing division is conceivable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the author's counter-scenario showing that near/far differentia can apply even when parts are equal.

Core Idea
Even if parts are equal, their distinguishing scope can differ—one may distinguish from all, another from some.

The author responds to the objection by building a hypothetical quiddity: a genus and differentia, where the genus itself is composed of two equal things. Each of those equal things can serve as a differentia: one distinguishes the genus from all existential partners (making it near for the higher quiddity), the other only from some (making it far). Thus the division is conceivable even for existential partners.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
We have a quiddity composed of a genus (jins) and a differentia (fasl). That genus itself is composed of two equal things (amrān mutasāwiyān).
The scenario is hypothetical to show conceivability.
2
Identify the two equal things as potential differentiae
Each of the two equal things becomes a differentia for the genus against existential partners. They also distinguish the higher quiddity from some existential partners.
Each has a different scope of distinction.
3
Determine near and far
The differentia that distinguishes the quiddity from all existential partners is near (qarīb); the one that distinguishes from only some is far (baʿīd).
This shows the division is coherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعترض عليه بأن قواعد الفن عامة شاملة لجميع المفهومات سواء كانت محققة الوجود في الخارج أو لا، فلايكون تحقق الوجود مقتضياً لتخصيص البحث به، فالصواب أن يقال : اعتبار الانقسام إلى القريب والبعيد لا يتصور في الفصول المميزة عن المشاركات الوجودية، فإنّ الماهية إذا تركبت من أمور متساوية كان تمييز كل واحد منها للماهية كتمييز الآخر لها، فلا يمكن عد بعضها مميزاً قريباً وبعضها مميزاً بعيداً ، وإلا يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجّح، فلذلك خص اعتبار الانقسام إلى القريب والبعيد بالفصول المميزة عن المشاركات الجنسية. ويرد عليه أن الانقسام إليهما يتصوّر في تلك الفصول أيضاً، فإنا إذا فرضنا ماهية مركبة من جنس وفصل، وفرضنا ذلك الجنس مركبا من أمرين متساويين، كان كلّ واحد من الأمرين المتساويين فصلاً مميزاً لذلك الجنس عن المشاركات الوجودية ومميّزاً لتلك الماهية عن بعض المشاركات الوجودية فقد وجد أحوال الفصول المميزة عن المشاركات الوجودية مختلفة في التميز، فحينئذ يمكن أن يقال : الفصل المميز للماهية عما يشاركها في الوجود إن ميزها عن جميع المشاركات، فهو فصل قريب لها، وإن ميزها عن بعضها فهو فصل بعيد لها.
شرح الرسالة الشمسية
المميز في الوجود ليس متحقق الوجود، بل هو مبني على احتمال يذكر. وربما يمكن أن يستدل على بطلانه بأن يقال لو تركبت ماهية حقيقة من أمرين متساويين فإما أن لا يحتاج أحدهما إلى الآخر – وهـو محـال : ضرورة وجوب احتياج بعض أجزاء الماهية الحقيقية إلى البعض - أو يحتاج، فإن احتاج كل منهما إلى الآخر يلزم الدور، وإلا يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجّح لأنهما ذاتيان متساويان، فاحتياج أحدهما إلى الآخر ليس أولى من احتياج الآخر إليه.
أو يقال : لوتركب جنس عال - كالجوهر مثلاً - من أمرين متساويين فأحدهما إن كان عرضاً فيلزم تقوم الجوهر بالعرض – وهو محال – وإن كان جوهراً : فإما أن يكون الجوهر نفسه، فيلزم أن يكون الكل نفس جزئه - وأنه محال - أو داخلاً فيه - وهو أيضاً محال لامتناع تركب الشيء من نفسه ومن غيره - أو خارجاً عنه، فيكون عارضاً له؛ لكن ذلك الجزء ليس عارضاً لنفسه، بل يكون العارض بالحقيقة هو الجزء الآخر ، فلا يكون العارض بتمامه عارضاً - وأنه محال – فلينظر في هذا المقام فإنه من مطارح الأذكياء.
فالأولى الاقتصار على ماذكره الشارح، فإن تحقق الوجود يقتضي زيادة الاعتناء به، فربما يقتصر في بعض المباحث على ماذكره، ويحال معرفة ماعداه على المقايسة به وأما التعريفات فالأولى بها شمولها للكل (شريف).
An objection was raised against it: that the rules of the art are general, encompassing all concepts whether they have realized existence externally or not, so the realization of existence does not necessitate restricting the inquiry to it. Rather, the correct thing to say is: the consideration of division into near and far is not conceivable in the differentiae that distinguish from existential partners. For if a quiddity is composed of equal things, the distinction of each one of them for the quiddity is like the distinction of the other for it, so it is impossible to count some as near differentia and some as far differentia; otherwise circularity without a preponderant factor would follow. Therefore, the consideration of division into near and far was restricted to the differentiae distinguishing from generic partners. It is replied to him: that the division into them is conceivable in those differentia as well. For if we suppose a quiddity composed of a genus and a differentia, and suppose that genus composed of two equal things, then each of the two equal things would be a differentia distinguishing that genus from existential partners and distinguishing that quiddity from some existential partners, so the states of the differentiae distinguishing from existential partners would be found different in distinguishing. Then it can be said: the differentia that distinguishes the quiddity from what shares existence with it, if it distinguishes it from all partners, then it is a near differentia for it, and if it distinguishes it from some partners, then it is a far differentia for it. Commentary on the Shamsiyyah: The differentia distinguishing in existence is not real existence; rather, it is based on a possible mention. And perhaps it can be argued for its invalidity by saying: if a real quiddity were composed of two equal things, then either one does not need the other – and that is impossible due to the necessity of some parts of the real quiddity needing others – or it needs. If each needs the other, a circle follows; otherwise, preponderance without a preponderant factor follows because they are essential and equal, so one's need of the other is not more appropriate than the other's need of it. Or it is said: if a high genus, like substance, were composed of two equal things, then if one of them is an accident, the subsistence of substance by accident would follow – which is impossible. And if it is a substance, then either it is the substance itself, so the whole would be the same as its part – which is impossible – or it is included in it, which is also impossible due to the impossibility of composition of a thing from itself and another – or external to it, so it would be an accident to it; but that part is not an accident to itself, rather the accident in reality is the other part, so the accident would not be an accident in its entirety – and that is impossible. So consider this position, for it is among the intellectual challenges. The best is to restrict to what the commentator mentioned, for the realization of existence necessitates increased attention to it. Perhaps in some investigations one may restrict to what he mentioned, and reference knowledge of what is beyond to analogy. As for definitions, the best is that they be universal. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. It begins with an objection against the earlier division restricting near/far differentiae to those distinguishing from generic partners. The objector argues that the rules of logic are general, so the division should apply to all concepts regardless of external existence. He claims that if a quiddity is composed of equal things (أمور متساوية), the distinguishing power of each is equal, so you cannot call some near and some far; thus the division only makes sense for generic partners. The author then responds (يرد عليه) by constructing a scenario: suppose a quiddity has a genus and differentia, and that genus itself is composed of two equal things. Each of those equal things can serve as a differentia for the genus against existential partners, and also distinguish the quiddity from some existential partners. Then one can be near (if it distinguishes from all partners) and the other far (if only some). This shows the division is conceivable. The passage then includes a marginal note from 'Sharif' arguing that it's better to stick with the commentator's original restriction, because realized existence warrants extra attention, and other cases can be handled by analogy. Additionally, a proof is given against the possibility of a real quiddity composed of two equal things, using arguments of circularity and preponderance, and another proof using the example of substance and accident. The passage ends with a recommendation.

The Deeper Proof – Impossibility of a Quiddity Composed of Equal Things
Proof of impossibility of a quiddity composed of equal essential parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the proof that a real quiddity cannot be composed of two equal essential parts and identify the logical steps.

Core Idea
Two equal parts cannot both be essential without circularity or arbitrary preference.

The margin note (attributed to Sharif) provides a proof that the scenario of a quiddity composed of equal things is actually impossible. It uses two arguments: first, if the parts are equal, either each needs the other (leading to circularity) or one does not need the other (leading to preponderance without preponderant). Second, using the example of a high genus like substance composed of two equal parts leads to absurdities involving accidents. These proofs show the scenario cannot be actual, so the response's scenario is only hypothetical.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first proof
Assume a real quiddity composed of two equal things. Consider mutual need: if each needs the other, circularity (dawr) results. If one does not need the other, then there is preponderance without preponderant (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). Both are impossible.
This reductio shows the assumption is false.
2
Understand the second proof
Take a high genus (al-jins al-ʿālī) like substance (jawhar) composed of two equal things. Each must be either accident or substance. If accident, then substance subsists by accident (impossible). If substance, then either it is the same substance (whole same as part), or included (composition of thing with itself), or external (accident). All lead to absurdities.
This proof uses the nature of substance and accident.
3
Evaluate the implications
These proofs show that the scenario of equal components cannot correspond to reality. Therefore, the response's scenario is purely hypothetical and does not actually occur in real definitions.
Sharif recommends restricting to the original commentator's approach.

Refining the Division of Necessary Concomitants
Author presents an objection about the middle term and refines the division of necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct classification of necessary concomitants (self-evident vs. non-self-evident) and explain why defining non-self-evident solely by need for a middle term leads to a third category.

Core Idea
Not all non-self-evident concomitants need a middle term; some rely on intuition or sense.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle problem in the classification of necessary concomitants (lawazim al-mahiyya). He notes that if we define 'non-self-evident' as requiring a middle term, we miss those concomitants that are not self-evident from mere conception but are known through intuition, experience, or senses. The refinement ensures that every necessary concomitant is either self-evident (bayyin) or non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin), with the latter further divided into theoretical (needing a middle term) and other immediate types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial classification
Note that the middle term (wasat) is defined as what accompanies 'because' (li'anna). Initially, non-self-evident is understood as needing a middle term. This gives a binary: self-evident (bayyin) and non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin).
This is the definition the author will critique.
2
Understand the objection
The objection: if non-self-evident means 'needs a middle term', then some necessary concomitants are not self-evident but also do not need a middle term (they rely on intuition, experience, or senses). This would create a third category, breaking the binary.
The middle term is not the only way to know a necessary connection.
3
Examine the refined definition of non-self-evident
The author redefines non-self-evident as: that for which conceiving the necessary and its subject is not sufficient for certainty. This definition does not require a middle term; it can be satisfied by other means.
This ensures the binary division holds.
4
Explore the subdivision of non-self-evident
Non-self-evident is divided into theoretical (nazari, needs a middle term) and a different type of self-evident (badihi, known through intuition, experience, or senses). This preserves the binary while accommodating the objection.
The author uses 'self-evident' in two senses: primary self-evident and other immediate types.
5
Check completeness of the refined classification
Verify that every necessary concomitant is either bayyin (evident from conception) or ghayr bayyin (not evident from conception). The latter includes both theoretical and experiential types. No third category remains.
This is the goal of the refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا نظر: وهو أن الوسط - على ما فسره القوم - ما يقترن بقولنا: «لأنه» حين يقال: «لأنه كذا»؛ مثلاً إذا قلنا: «العالم محدث لأنه متغير» فالمقارن لقولنا «لأنه» - وهو «المتغير» - وسط، وليس يلزم من افتقار اللزوم إلى وسط أنه يكفي فيه مجرد تصور اللازم والملزوم، لجواز توقفه على شيء آخر - من حدس أو تجربة أو إحساس أو غير ذلك - فلو اعتبرنا الافتقار إلى الوسط في مفهوم «غير البين» لم ينحصر لازم الماهية في البين وغيره، لوجود قسم ثالث.
وقد يقال: «البين على اللازم الذي يلزم من تصور ملزومه تصوره»، يعني أن لازم الماهية إذا لم يكن تصورهما كافيا في الجزم باللزوم بينهما وجب أن يتوقف الجزم به على أمر مغاير لتصورهما، ولا يجب أن يكون ذلك الأمر الموقوف عليه هو الوسط، بل يجوز أن يكون شيئا آخر، كالحدس وأخواته. وتوضيحه أن المحتاج إلى الوسط بالمعنى المذكور يكون قضية نظرية، والذي يكفي تصور طرفيه في الجزم به يكون قضية أولية، فكأنه قال: اللزوم الذي بين الماهية ولازمها إما بديهي أولي وإما كسبي نظري؛ فورد أنه يجوز أن لا يكون نظريا ولا أوليا، بل يكون بديهيا مغايرا للأولى كالحدسي والتجربي والحسي، فمن أراد حصر لازم الماهية في البين وغيره وجب أن لا يعتبر في مفهوم «غير البين» الاحتياج إلى الوسط، بل يكتفي بعدم كون تصور اللازم مع تصور الملزوم كافيا في الجزم باللزوم، وحينئذ يظهر الانحصار ويكون غير البين منقسما إلى نظري يفتقر إلى الوسط وإلى بديهي يفتقر إلى أمر آخر سوى تصور الطرفين والوسط.
And here is a consideration: that the middle term - according to what the people have explained - is that which accompanies our statement 'because' when it is said: 'because it is such'; for example, when we say: 'The world is originated because it is changing' then that which accompanies our statement 'because' - which is 'changing' - is the middle term. And it does not necessarily follow from the need of the necessary connection for a middle term that merely conceiving the necessary and the necessitating is sufficient in it, because it is possible for it to depend on something else - from intuition or experience or sense or other than that. So if we considered the need for a middle term in the concept of 'non-self-evident', the necessary concomitant of the essence would not be confined to the self-evident and non-self-evident, due to the existence of a third division.
And it is said: 'The self-evident is the necessary concomitant whose conception follows from the conception of its necessitating,' meaning that if the conception of the two [the necessary and the necessitating] is not sufficient for the mind to be certain of the necessary connection between them, then the certainty of it must depend on something different from the conception of the two, and it is not necessary that that on which it depends is the middle term; rather, it is permissible that it be something else, like intuition and its likes. And its clarification is that that which needs the middle term in the mentioned sense is a theoretical proposition, and that whose two extremes are sufficient in conception for certainty in it is a primary proposition. So it is as if he said: The necessary connection between the essence and its necessary concomitant is either primary self-evident or acquired theoretical. Then it was objected that it is permissible that it be neither theoretical nor primary, but rather self-evident different from the primary, like the intuitive, experiential, and sensory. So whoever wants to confine the necessary concomitant of the essence to the self-evident and non-self-evident must not consider in the concept of 'non-self-evident' the need for a middle term; rather, he suffices with the fact that the conception of the necessary along with the conception of the necessitating is not sufficient for certainty of the necessary connection. And then the confinement appears, and the non-self-evident is divided into theoretical (which needs a middle term) and self-evident (which needs something else besides the conception of the two extremes and the middle term).
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its resolution concerning the classification of necessary concomitants (lawazim al-mahiyya). The author first defines the middle term (al-wasat) as that which accompanies the word 'because' in statements like 'The world is originated because it is changing.' The objection is that if we define 'non-self-evident' (ghayr bayyin) as that which requires a middle term, then there would be a third category: those necessary concomitants that are not self-evident but also do not need a middle term; they depend on something else like intuition, experience, or sense perception. The author then refines the definition: 'self-evident' (al-bayyin) is a necessary that is known merely by conceiving its subject (al-malzum). For non-self-evident, one should not restrict it to needing a middle term; rather, it is simply that conceiving the necessary and its subject is insufficient for certainty. This non-self-evident is then divided into two: theoretical (nazari), which requires a middle term, and primary self-evident (badihi) of a different kind (e.g., intuitive, experiential, sensory), which does not require a middle term but depends on something else. This ensures that the necessary concomitant of an essence is either bayyin or ghayr bayyin, with the latter being further subdivided.

Key Terms
وسط
لازم الماهية
بين
غير بين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct classification of the necessary concomitant of an essence?

B) Self-evident and non-self-evident, with non-self-evident including both theoretical and other types.
C) Self-evident, theoretical, and intuitive.
D) Only self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The necessary concomitant of an essence is either self-evident (known upon conception) or non-self-evident, and non-self-evident includes both theoretical (middle term) and intuitive/experiential types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If non-self-evident, identify whether it is theoretical (needs a syllogism) or known through another means (e.g., revelation).

External vs. Mental Necessary Concomitants
Author distinguishes external vs. mental necessary concomitants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external and mental necessary concomitants and identify examples of each.

Core Idea
External necessity binds existence; mental necessity binds conception.

Implicational signification (dalāla iltizāmiyya) relies on necessary concomitants. But not all necessities are equal: some are rooted in the external world, others in the mind. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments that rely on implications, especially in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Notice that the author divides necessary concomitants into two categories: external (lāzim khārijī) and mental (lāzim dhihnī). The first concerns existence in the external world; the second concerns conception in the mind.
2
Analyze the external example
The passage gives 'origination (huduth) for the body' as an external necessary concomitant. Ask: 'Is it possible for a body to exist without being originated?' No, because every body in the external world is temporally originated. Therefore, origination is an external concomitant.
External concomitants hold in reality regardless of whether we think about them.
3
Understand the mental example
The passage begins to explain mental necessity: it is impossible to conceive the second without the first. For instance, you cannot conceive 'human' without conceiving 'animal'. This is a mental concomitant because it is a relation between concepts, not between things in the external world.
Mental concomitants depend on the structure of concepts, not physical existence.
4
Test the distinction
For any claimed necessary concomitant, ask: 'Is it impossible for the subject to exist without this property in the external world?' If yes, it is external. If it is only impossible to conceive the subject without the property, it is mental.
This test prevents confusion between real connections and purely conceptual ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا هو اللازم الذهني المعتبر في الدلالة الالتزامية، فإن لزوم شيء لشيء إما أن يكون بحسب الوجود الخارجي على معنى أنه يمتنع وجود الشيء الثاني في الخارج منفكاً عن الشيء الأول، كالحدوث للجسم، فإن وجود الجسم يمتنع بدون الحدوث، فالحدوث لازم خارجي للجسم، ويسمى لزوماً خارجياً؛ وإما أن يكون بحسب الوجود الذهني على معنى أنه يمتنع حصول الشيء الثاني في الذهن منفكاً عن حصول الشيء الأول فيه، وحاصله أنه يمتنع إدراك الثاني.
This is the mental necessary concomitant considered in implicational signification. For the necessity of one thing for another is either with respect to external existence, meaning that the existence of the second thing in the external world is impossible separate from the first thing, like origination for the body; indeed, the existence of the body is impossible without origination, so origination is an external necessary concomitant of the body, and it is called external necessity. Or it is with respect to mental existence, meaning that the occurrence of the second thing in the mind is impossible separate from the occurrence of the first thing in it; its import is that it is impossible to perceive the second.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two types of necessary concomitants (luzum) relevant to implicational signification (dalala iltizamiyya). The first is external necessity (luzum khariji), which applies when one thing cannot exist in the external world without another. An example given is origination (huduth) for the body: a body cannot exist without being originated, so origination is an external necessary concomitant of the body. The second is mental necessity (luzum dhihni), which applies when one thing cannot be conceived in the mind without conceiving another. The passage begins to explain that it is impossible to perceive the second without the first, though the text cuts off. This distinction is important for understanding how implications work in logic: some implications are based on real-world existential connections, while others are based on conceptual or mental connections.

Key Terms
لازم خارجي
لازم ذهني
دلالة التزامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The idea of 'heat' for 'fire'.

Key Takeaway
Necessary concomitants are divided into external (based on existence) and mental (based on conception), and this division underlies the proper use of implicational signification in Islamic logic.

Two Senses of 'Clear' (Bayyin) in Necessary Concomitants
Definition and defense of the division of necessary concomitants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general and specific senses of 'clear concomitant' and evaluate whether a necessary concomitant of essence can be non-clear.

Core Idea
Being present in the mind does not mean being perceived.

This lesson refines a key concept in Islamic logic: the division of necessary concomitants into clear and non-clear. The author clarifies that 'clear' has two meanings: a general sense where both terms suffice to affirm entailment, and a specific sense where the essence alone suffices to conceive the concomitant. He then defends the division against an objection rooted in confusing mental presence with awareness.

Guided Path
1
Define the two senses of bayyin
Identify the general sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm): conception of both the essence and the concomitant suffices to affirm the entailment. The specific sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass): conception of the essence alone suffices to conceive the concomitant.
The passage gives both definitions explicitly.
2
Understand the division of lazim al-mahiyya
The necessary concomitant of essence (lazim al-mahiyya) is divided into clear (general sense) and non-clear (ghayr bayyin). This division is based on whether the two sides' conception suffices for affirming the concomitance.
The non-clear concomitant is one where more analysis is needed to affirm the entailment.
3
Identify the objection
An objector argues: since lazim al-mahiyya must be a mental concomitant (exists in the mind when the essence is conceived), it must be clear in the specific sense. This would eliminate the non-clear category, making the division invalid.
The objection hinges on equating mental existence with epistemic awareness.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author uses the example of a triangle: its angles equal two right angles is a necessary concomitant, but one can conceive the triangle without being aware of that property. Thus, mental presence does not entail perception. The division stands.
This shows why the concomitant can be non-clear even though it is mentally present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال «البين على اللازم الذي يلزم من تصور ملزومه تصوره». واعترض عليه بأن المعتبر في كون اللزوم بيناً هو كون تصورهما كافيين في الجزم باللزوم، والمعتبر في كون اللازم بيناً بالمعنى الأخص هو كون تصور الملزوم كافياً في تصور اللازم. ثم إن لازم الماهية ينقسم إلى بين بالمعنى الأعم وهو ما يكفي تصور طرفيه في الجزم باللزوم، وغير بين وهو ما لا يكفي. ثم إن لازم الماهية من حيث هي هي يجب أن يكون لازماً ذهنياً، لأنه إذا وجدت الماهية في الذهن وجب أن يوجد ذلك اللازم فيه أيضاً، فيكون لازم الماهية لازماً ذهنياً قطعاً، فيكون بيناً بالمعنى الأخص، فلا يجوز انقسامه إلى اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم وغير البين. قلت: الواجب في لازم الماهية أن يكون بحيث إذا وجدت الماهية في الذهن كانت متصفة به ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون اللازم مدركاً مشعوراً به، فإن ماهية المثلث إذا وجدت في الذهن كانت موصوفة بكون زواياها الثلاث مساوية لقائمتين، ومع ذلك يمكن أن لا يكون للذهن شعور بمفهوم المساواة المذكورة – فضلا عن الجزم بثبوتها لماهية المثلث - فليس كل ماكان حاصلاً للماهية المدركة في الذهن يجب أن يكون مدركاً، فإن كون الماهية مدركة صفة حاصلة لها هناك، مع أنه لا يجب الشعور به، وإلا لزم من إدراك أمر واحد إدراك أمور غير متناهية، بل يجوز أن يكون لازم الماهية بحيث يلزم من تصورهما الجزم باللزوم بينهما، وأن لا يكون كذلك؛ فصح الانقسام إلى البين بالمعنى الأعم وغير البين؛ ويجوز أن يكون بحيث يلزم من تصور الملزوم – أي الماهية – تصوره فيكون بيناً بالمعنى الأخص، وأن لا يكون بهذه الحيثية.
And it is said: 'Clear (bayyin) with respect to the concomitant (lazim) is that which follows from the conception of its correlative (malzum) its conception.' It is objected that what is considered in the concomitance being clear is that their [both] conception is sufficient for affirming the concomitance, and what is considered in the concomitant being clear in the most specific sense is that the conception of the correlative is sufficient for the conception of the concomitant. Then the necessary concomitant of essence (lazim al-mahiyya) is divided into clear in the more general sense, which is that whose two sides' conception suffices for affirming the concomitance, and non-clear, which does not suffice. Then the necessary concomitant of essence, considered in itself, must be a mental concomitant (lazim dhihni), because when the essence exists in the mind, that concomitant must also exist in it, so the necessary concomitant of essence is necessarily a mental concomitant, and thus it is clear in the most specific sense, and thus its division into clear in the more general sense and non-clear is not allowed. I say: What is necessary in the necessary concomitant of essence is that it be such that when the essence exists in the mind, it is characterized by it; but it does not follow from that that the concomitant is perceived or cognized. For the essence of triangle, when it exists in the mind, is characterized by its three angles being equal to two right angles, and yet the mind may not have awareness of the concept of that equality – let alone affirmation of its subsistence for the essence of triangle. So not everything that is present for the essence perceived in the mind must be perceived, for the fact that the essence is perceived is a quality that is present for it there, although it is not necessary to be aware of it, otherwise the perception of one thing would entail the perception of infinite things. Rather, it is possible that the necessary concomitant of essence be such that the conception of the two [i.e., the essence and the concomitant] necessitates affirmation of the concomitance between them, and that it be not so; so the division into clear in the more general sense and non-clear is correct. And it is possible that it be such that the conception of the correlative – i.e., the essence – necessitates its conception, in which case it is clear in the most specific sense, and that it be not with this property.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the classification of necessary concomitants. It begins by presenting a definition of 'clear' (bayyin) in two ways: clear in the general sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm) requires only that the conception of both the essence and the concomitant suffices to affirm the entailment; clear in the specific sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass) requires that the conception of the essence alone suffices to conceive the concomitant. Then the author divides the necessary concomitant of essence (lazim al-mahiyya) into clear (general) and non-clear. However, an objection is raised: if the necessary concomitant of essence is a mental concomitant, then it must be clear in the specific sense because whenever the essence is conceptualized, the concomitant is present. This would eliminate the possibility of a non-clear concomitant. The response clarifies that 'being present in the mind' does not imply 'being perceived.' For example, the property that a triangle's angles sum to two right angles is a necessary concomitant of the triangle's essence in the mind, but one may conceive the triangle without being aware of that property. Thus, the concomitant is not clear in the specific sense, preserving the division into clear (general) and non-clear. The passage thus refines the understanding of necessary concomitance by distinguishing between ontological concomitance and epistemic awareness.

لازم الماهية
اللازم الذهني

Key Takeaway

Defining Universals: Descriptions or Limits?
Critique of the claim that universal definitions are descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique the claim that definitions of the five universals are mere descriptions by arguing that universals are mental constructs.

Core Idea
For mental constructs, definition is limit, not description – there is no hidden essence.

In this passage, the author challenges the common assumption that the definitions of the five universals given earlier are only descriptions (rusum). He argues that since universals are mental constructs (umur i'tibariyya), their definitions directly capture their concepts, making them actual limits (hudud). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The author is responding to the view that definitions of universals are descriptions (rusum) rather than limits (hudud). Find where he states this.
2
Locate the core reason
The author says: 'because the universals are mental considerations; their concepts are obtained first and names placed for them, so they have no meanings other than those concepts.' This is the key premise.
3
Evaluate the logic
If a concept has no extra-mental reality, then any definition that captures the concept is exhaustive – it is a limit, not a description. Explain why this follows.
4
Conclude the critique
The author rejects the cautious approach of calling them descriptions; he insists they are limits. Note the implication: ignorance that they are limits does not make them descriptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما كانت هذه التعريفات رسوماً للكليات لجواز أن يكون لها ماهيات وراء تلك المفهومات ملزومات مساوية لها، فحيث لم يتحقق ذلك أطلق عليها اسم «الرسم». وهو بمعزل عن التحقيق، لأن الكليات أمور اعتبارية حصلت مفهوماتها أولاً ووضعت أسماؤها بإزائها، فليس لها معان غير تلك المفهومات، فتكون هي حدوداً لها ) ، على أن عدم العلم بأنها حدود لا يوجب العلم بأنها رسوم ؛ فكان المناسب ذكر التعريف الذي هو من الحد والرسم. وفي تمثيل الكليات بالناطق والضاحك والماشي - لا بالنطق والضحك والمشي التي هي مبادئها - فائدة، وهي أن المعتبر في حمل الكلي إما حقيقية - أي موجودة في الأعيان - وإما اعتبارية - أي موجودة في الذهن : أما الحقيقيات فالتميّز بين ذاتيّاتها وعرضيّاتها في غاية الإشكال، لالتباس الجنس بالعرض العام والفصل بالخاصة، فتعسر التمييز بين حدودها ورسومها المسماة بالحدود والرسوم الحقيقية، وأما الاعتباريات فلا إشكال فيها لأن كل ما هو داخل في مفهومها فهو ذاتي لها ، إما جنس إن كان مشتركاً وإما فصل إن كان مميزاً ولم يكن مشتركاً، وكلّ ماليس داخلاً في مفهومها فهو عرضي لها، فلا اشتباه بين حدودها ورسومها المسماة بالحدود والرسوم الاسمية (شریف).
And these definitions are only descriptions (rusum) of the universals because it is possible that they have realities (mahiyyat) behind those concepts that are concomitant and coextensive with them, and when that is not actualized, the term 'description' is applied to them. But this is far from verification, because the universals are mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya); their concepts are obtained first and their names are placed for them, so they have no meanings other than those concepts. Thus they are limits (hudud) for them. Moreover, ignorance that they are limits does not necessitate knowledge that they are descriptions. So it would have been more appropriate to mention the definition that is both limit and description. And in their exemplification of the universals by 'rational', 'laughing', and 'walking' - not by 'rationality', 'laughter', and 'walking' which are their principles - there is a benefit: that what is considered in predicating the universal is that realities are either real (i.e., existent in external objects) or mental (i.e., existent in the mind). As for real things, distinguishing between their essentials and accidents is extremely problematic due to the confusion of genus with common accident and differentia with special characteristic, so distinguishing between their limits and descriptions, which are called real limits and real descriptions, is difficult. As for mental considerations, there is no problem because everything that enters into their concept is essential to them, either as a genus if it is shared or as a differentia if it is distinguishing and not shared, and everything that does not enter into their concept is accidental to them. Hence there is no confusion between their limits and descriptions, which are called nominal limits and nominal descriptions.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage critiques the view that the definitions of the five universals given earlier are merely descriptions (rusum) rather than true limits (hudud). The author begins by stating that these definitions were called descriptions only because it was assumed there might be realities (mahiyyat) behind the concepts that are concomitant and coextensive, and if those realities are not known, the definitions are called descriptions. The author strongly rejects this, saying it is far from the truth ('huwa bi-ma'zil 'an al-tahqiq'). The reason: the universals are mental constructs (umur i'tibariyya); their concepts are immediately grasped and names are assigned to those concepts. There are no extra-mental meanings behind them. Therefore, the definitions that specify those concepts are actually limits (hudud) of the universals, not descriptions. Ignorance that they are limits does not prove they are descriptions. Then the commentary (by al-Sharif) adds: a more appropriate approach would be to call them definitions that participate in both limit and description. Additionally, there is a benefit in the authors' use of concrete examples like 'rational' (natiq), 'laughing' (dahik), and 'walking' (mashi) rather than the abstract principles 'rationality', 'laughter', 'walking'. The benefit is that when predicating a universal, we must consider whether the reality is external or mental. For external realities (haqiqiyyat), distinguishing essential from accidental is very difficult because genera and common accidents, differentiae and special characteristics get confused. But for mental considerations (i'tibariyyat), there is no confusion: anything inside the concept is essential (either genus if shared, or differentia if unique), anything outside is accidental. Thus, the distinction between nominal limits and nominal descriptions is clear.

أمر اعتباري
ماهية

Concrete vs Abstract Examples in Defining Mental Constructs
Benefit of concrete examples in defining universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians use concrete examples (e.g., nāṭiq, ḍāḥik, māshī) rather than abstract principles (e.g., nuṭq, ḍiḥk, mashy) when defining universals.

Core Idea
Concrete examples avoid confusing essential and accidental in mental constructs.

In the latter part of the passage, the commentator al-Sharif highlights a pedagogical benefit in the logicians' choice of examples. By using concrete predicates like 'rational' and 'laughing' rather than abstract nouns like 'rationality' and 'laughter', they clarify that for mental constructs, everything inside the concept is essential, and everything outside is accidental. This prevents confusion between genus and common accident, which is especially problematic for real external things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contrast
The passage notes that logicians use 'rational' (nāṭiq) not 'rationality' (nuṭq), and similarly for other universals. Locate these examples in the text.
2
Understand the reason
For real external things (ḥaqīqiyyāt), distinguishing essential from accidental is hard because genus and common accident look similar. For mental constructs (iʿtibāriyyāt), it is easy: whatever is part of the concept is essential.
3
Apply the distinction
Concrete examples (mushtaqqāt) make the concept clear; abstract principles (maṣādir) are the bases (mabādiʾ) and not directly the universals being predicated.
4
Conclude the benefit
The choice of examples shows the logicians' awareness that for mental constructs, the concept itself is the essence, guiding the use of concrete predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما كانت هذه التعريفات رسوماً للكليات لجواز أن يكون لها ماهيات وراء تلك المفهومات ملزومات مساوية لها، فحيث لم يتحقق ذلك أطلق عليها اسم «الرسم». وهو بمعزل عن التحقيق، لأن الكليات أمور اعتبارية حصلت مفهوماتها أولاً ووضعت أسماؤها بإزائها، فليس لها معان غير تلك المفهومات، فتكون هي حدوداً لها ) ، على أن عدم العلم بأنها حدود لا يوجب العلم بأنها رسوم ؛ فكان المناسب ذكر التعريف الذي هو من الحد والرسم. وفي تمثيل الكليات بالناطق والضاحك والماشي - لا بالنطق والضحك والمشي التي هي مبادئها - فائدة، وهي أن المعتبر في حمل الكلي إما حقيقية - أي موجودة في الأعيان - وإما اعتبارية - أي موجودة في الذهن : أما الحقيقيات فالتميّز بين ذاتيّاتها وعرضيّاتها في غاية الإشكال، لالتباس الجنس بالعرض العام والفصل بالخاصة، فتعسر التمييز بين حدودها ورسومها المسماة بالحدود والرسوم الحقيقية، وأما الاعتباريات فلا إشكال فيها لأن كل ما هو داخل في مفهومها فهو ذاتي لها ، إما جنس إن كان مشتركاً وإما فصل إن كان مميزاً ولم يكن مشتركاً، وكلّ ماليس داخلاً في مفهومها فهو عرضي لها، فلا اشتباه بين حدودها ورسومها المسماة بالحدود والرسوم الاسمية (شریف).
And these definitions are only descriptions (rusum) of the universals because it is possible that they have realities (mahiyyat) behind those concepts that are concomitant and coextensive with them, and when that is not actualized, the term 'description' is applied to them. But this is far from verification, because the universals are mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya); their concepts are obtained first and their names are placed for them, so they have no meanings other than those concepts. Thus they are limits (hudud) for them. Moreover, ignorance that they are limits does not necessitate knowledge that they are descriptions. So it would have been more appropriate to mention the definition that is both limit and description. And in their exemplification of the universals by 'rational', 'laughing', and 'walking' - not by 'rationality', 'laughter', and 'walking' which are their principles - there is a benefit: that what is considered in predicating the universal is that realities are either real (i.e., existent in external objects) or mental (i.e., existent in the mind). As for real things, distinguishing between their essentials and accidents is extremely problematic due to the confusion of genus with common accident and differentia with special characteristic, so distinguishing between their limits and descriptions, which are called real limits and real descriptions, is difficult. As for mental considerations, there is no problem because everything that enters into their concept is essential to them, either as a genus if it is shared or as a differentia if it is distinguishing and not shared, and everything that does not enter into their concept is accidental to them. Hence there is no confusion between their limits and descriptions, which are called nominal limits and nominal descriptions.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage critiques the view that the definitions of the five universals given earlier are merely descriptions (rusum) rather than true limits (hudud). The author begins by stating that these definitions were called descriptions only because it was assumed there might be realities (mahiyyat) behind the concepts that are concomitant and coextensive, and if those realities are not known, the definitions are called descriptions. The author strongly rejects this, saying it is far from the truth ('huwa bi-ma'zil 'an al-tahqiq'). The reason: the universals are mental constructs (umur i'tibariyya); their concepts are immediately grasped and names are assigned to those concepts. There are no extra-mental meanings behind them. Therefore, the definitions that specify those concepts are actually limits (hudud) of the universals, not descriptions. Ignorance that they are limits does not prove they are descriptions. Then the commentary (by al-Sharif) adds: a more appropriate approach would be to call them definitions that participate in both limit and description. Additionally, there is a benefit in the authors' use of concrete examples like 'rational' (natiq), 'laughing' (dahik), and 'walking' (mashi) rather than the abstract principles 'rationality', 'laughter', 'walking'. The benefit is that when predicating a universal, we must consider whether the reality is external or mental. For external realities (haqiqiyyat), distinguishing essential from accidental is very difficult because genera and common accidents, differentiae and special characteristics get confused. But for mental considerations (i'tibariyyat), there is no confusion: anything inside the concept is essential (either genus if shared, or differentia if unique), anything outside is accidental. Thus, the distinction between nominal limits and nominal descriptions is clear.

The Three Aspects of Every Universal
Author explains why the example 'animal' is used and distinguishes the three types of universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of universal (natural, logical, mental) and explain why only the natural universal's external existence is briefly discussed in logic.

Core Idea
Every universal has three aspects: natural (external existence), logical (concept), mental (abstraction).

This passage from a commentary on al-Shamsiyya clarifies why the author used 'animal' as an example for the three universals. It shows that the threefold consideration applies to every universal concept, not just animal. More importantly, it explains why only the natural universal's external existence is asserted in logic, while the other two are left to metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three universals mentioned
Find in the Arabic the terms for natural universal, logical universal, and mental universal. Note that they are introduced as three considerations of any universal concept.
The terms appear after stating that the example 'animal' is not exclusive.
2
Extract the proof for the natural universal's existence
The author argues: 'this animal exists, animal is part of this animal, a part of an existing thing exists, so animal exists.' Trace this simple argument to understand why the natural universal is said to exist externally.
3
Understand why the other two universals are not discussed
The commentator says there is disagreement about the external existence of the logical and mental universals, and that investigation belongs to divine wisdom (metaphysics), not logic. Note the justification: a brief indication suffices for the natural universal, but lengthy discussion of the others has no benefit for logic.
The footnotes add that even some natural universals (like a partner of Allah) do not exist externally; the existence of the natural universal in general is a metaphysical issue.
4
Apply the threefold distinction to another universal
The author mentions that if we say 'man is a species', we get a natural species, logical species, and mental species. Think of another universal (e.g., genus 'animal') and imagine its three aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And he only said 'for example, animal' because the consideration of these three matters is not specific to animal nor to the concept of the universal, but rather encompasses all essences and concepts of universals, such that if we say: man is a species, we obtain a natural species, a logical species, and a mental species; likewise in genus and differentia and others. And the natural universal exists externally, because this animal exists, and 'animal' is a part of this existing animal, and a part of an existing thing exists, so animal exists; and it is the natural universal. As for the other two universals – that is, the logical universal and the mental universal – there is disagreement about their existence externally; and investigation into that is outside this art, because it is among the issues of divine wisdom which investigates the states of existents insofar as they exist, and this is shared between them and the natural universal, so there is no reason to mention it here and to refer them to another science. (1) He means: it may be that some natural universal exists in it, not that every natural universal exists externally, for among natural universals are what are impossible in existence, like the partner of Allah, and what are non-existent but possible, like the phoenix. (2) He intends by it that investigation into the existence of the natural universal is also outside the art, and it is among the issues of divine wisdom. (3) It was said: the reason is that explaining the existence of the natural universal suffices with a minimal indication, while knowledge of its existence is beneficial in examples clarifying the rules of the art, unlike the other two, for there the speech would be lengthy and no benefit, so the first was deemed good to mention and the other two left.

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on al-Shamsiyya, expanding on why the author of the original matn used 'animal' as an example. The commentator explains that the consideration of the three aspects of a universal (natural, logical, mental) applies to all essences, not just animal. He then clarifies that the natural universal exists externally, using the concrete example of 'animal' being part of an existing animal. The logical and mental universals are controversial; their existence is a metaphysical question, not a logical one. The footnotes refine this: the first notes that not all natural universals exist (some are impossible like a partner of Allah, some merely possible like the phoenix). The second notes that even the existence of the natural universal is metaphysical, but the third footnote justifies its mention because a brief indication suffices and helps illustrate logical rules.

Negations of Terms with Partial Generality Are Completely Exclusive
As a principle about negations of partially general terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two negated terms have any generality relationship, given the relationship of the original terms.

Core Idea
If two terms overlap partially, their negations have no overlap at all.

In classical Islamic logic, we often analyze the relationships between universal terms. A key finding is that when two terms have 'partial generality' (umum min wajh) – meaning they overlap in some instances but each applies without the other in others – their negations become completely mutually exclusive. This lesson unpacks that principle using the example of 'animal' and 'not-man' from al-Qutbi's commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original terms and their relationship
Take the example given: 'animal' and 'not-man'. Check if they have umum min wajh by finding instances where they coincide and where each applies without the other. They coincide in 'horse', animal applies without not-man in 'man', and not-man applies without animal in 'mineral'. Thus they have partial generality.
This step is crucial: not all pairs of terms have partial generality; some have absolute generality or complete exclusivity.
2
Negate both terms
Form the negations: 'not-animal' and 'man'. Now you have two new terms to analyze.
Negation here is the contradictory opposite, meaning everything that is not the term.
3
Check for any overlap between the negations
Ask: is there any instance that is both 'not-animal' and 'man'? By definition, a man is an animal, so nothing can be both. Therefore they are completely mutually exclusive (tabayun kulli).
The qualification 'kulli' (complete) means no instance whatsoever can satisfy both.
4
Generalize the principle
Now you can apply this to any pair of terms with partial generality: their negations will always be completely mutually exclusive, with no generality at all – neither absolute nor partial.
This holds regardless of the specific terms, as long as the original relation is truly umum min wajh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمران اللذان بينهما عموم من وجه ليس بين نقيضيهما عموم أصلاً - أي لا مطلقاً ولا من وجه - لأنّ هذا العموم - أي العموم من وجه - متحقق بين عين الأعم مطلقاً ونقيض الأخص، وليس بين نقيضيهما عموم - لا مطلقاً ولا من وجه :
اما تحقق العموم من وجه بينهما : فلأنهما يتصادقان في أخص آخر، ويصدق الأعم بدون نقيض الأخص في ذلك الأخـص وبالعكس في نقيض الأعم كالحيوان واللاإنسان، فإنّهما يجتمعان في الفرس، والحيوان يصدق بدون اللا إنسان في الإنسان، واللا إنسان بدون الحيوان في الجماد.
وأما أنه لا يكون بين نقيضيهما عموم أصلا : فللتباين الكلي بين نقيض الأعم وعين الأخص لامتناع صدقهما على «شيء»، فلايكون بينهما عموم أصلا. وإنما قيد التباين بالكلي) لأن التباين قديكون جزئياً وهو صدق كل
(۱) توضيحه أن مورد تحقق العموم من وجه هو بين عين الأعم من الشيء مطلقا ونقيض أخصه، مثل الحيوان والانسان، فبينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق، فيكون بين عين الأعم - «الحيوان» ونقيض الأخص - اللا إنسان»- عموم من وجه، إذ يتصادقان في الفرس ويصدق الحيوان بدون اللا إنسان في الإنسان، ويصدق اللا إنسان بدون الحيوان في الجماد.

The two terms between which there is generality in some respect (umum min wajh) — between their negations there is no generality at all — meaning neither absolutely nor in some respect — because this generality, i.e., generality in some respect, is realized between the very absolute general and the negation of the specific, and between their negations there is no generality — neither absolutely nor in some respect. As for the realization of generality in some respect between them: because they coincide in another more specific [term], and the general is true without the negation of the specific in that more specific, and conversely regarding the negation of the general, like 'animal' and 'not-man': they meet in 'horse', and 'animal' is true without 'not-man' in 'man', and 'not-man' without 'animal' in 'mineral'. As for that there is no generality at all between their negations: because of the complete mutual exclusivity (tabayun kulli) between the negation of the general and the very specific, since it is impossible for them to both be true of anything, so there is no generality between them at all. And the exclusivity was qualified as 'complete' because exclusivity may be partial, which is each being true of something [while not both]. (1) Its clarification: the locus of realization of generality in some respect is between the very absolute general of a thing and the negation of its specific, like 'animal' and 'man': between them is absolute generality and specificity. So between the very absolute general — 'animal' — and the negation of the specific — 'not-man' — there is generality in some respect, since they coincide in 'horse', and 'animal' is true without 'not-man' in 'man', and 'not-man' without 'animal' in 'mineral'. And by this explanation it becomes clear that there is no generality realized between their negations at all, for between the negation of the general of a thing and its specific — 'not-animal' and 'man' — there is complete mutual exclusivity, so no generality can be realized between them. And we have said that generality in some respect is realized between these two concepts. (b) (2) Its summary: if 'mutual exclusivity' were left unqualified and not restricted to 'complete', then from the existence of exclusivity between ...
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines and explains a key logical principle: when two terms have a relationship of 'generality in some respect' (umum min wajh) — meaning they overlap in some instances but not others — then the negations of these two terms are completely mutually exclusive (tabayun kulli), having no generality relationship whatsoever. The author begins by stating the principle, then explains why the generality in some respect holds between the absolute universal (like 'animal') and the negation of the specific (like 'not-man'), using the example of 'animal' and 'not-man': they coincide in 'horse' (since a horse is both an animal and not a man), and 'animal' applies without 'not-man' in the case of 'man' (since a man is an animal but not not-man), and 'not-man' applies without 'animal' in the case of 'mineral' (since a mineral is not a man and also not an animal). Thus there is partial overlap. However, when we take the negations of these two terms — i.e., 'not-animal' and 'man' — they are completely mutually exclusive because nothing can be both not-animal and man. So there is no generality at all between them. The author then qualifies 'mutual exclusivity' as 'complete' because exclusivity can be partial (where each term applies to some things but they do not overlap). He then provides a more detailed explanation (in the footnote) reiterating the same point. The last part (cut off) notes that if exclusivity were left unqualified as 'complete', then the conclusion would not follow.

تباين كلي

Critique of the Definition of Relative Particular
Criticizes the definition of idafi particular and explains mutual relation with idafi universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circularity in defining the relative particular and distinguish between real and relative universals and particulars.

Core Idea
Relative particular and relative universal are correlates; defining one with the other is circular.

This passage critically examines the definition of the relative particular (juz'i idafi), showing why common definitions lead to conceptual collapse or circularity. Understanding this critique sharpens your grasp of the taxonomy of universals and particulars in Islamic logic, ensuring that definitions remain logically sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed definition
Find the definition of relative particular being critiqued: 'that whose subsumption under something is possible.' Note that this is a tentative definition introduced with 'ولو قلنا' (if we say).
2
Apply the same logic to relative universal
See what the relative universal would be under this definition: 'that under which subsumption of something is possible.' This collapses into the real universal, losing the distinction.
The author shows that defining by possibility leads to symmetry, which is problematic.
3
Consider the horse example
Reflect on why a horse is not called a relative particular to man, even though it could be subsumed under the concept of man. This shows possibility alone is insufficient for the definition.
The example illustrates that actual subsumption, not mere possibility, is needed.
4
Understand the correlation (tadāyuf)
Grasp that the relative particular is synonymous with 'specific' (al-khāṣṣ) and the relative universal with 'general' (al-'āmm). They are correlates like father and son, conceived only together.
Correlates cannot be used in each other's definitions without circularity.
5
Examine the objection and response
Consider the objection that the definition uses 'the more general' (a'amm) rather than 'the general' ('amm). The passage implies this does not avoid the problem, as the relation remains correlative.
The objection is raised but not fully answered; the focus is on the inherent circularity.

ولو قلنا : الجزئي الإضافي ما أمكن اندراجه تحت شيء» كان الكلي الإضافي ما أمكن اندراج شيء تحته، فيكون أيضاً أخص من الكلّي الحقيقي، لكن بدرجة واحدة، ولا يصح أن يقال : «الجزئي الإضافي ما أمكن فرض اندراجه تحت شيء آخر حتى يلزم أنّ الكلّي الإضافي ما أمكن فرض اندراج شيء تحته، فيرجع إلى المعنى الحقيقي كمامر.
وإنما لم يصح تفسير الجزئي الإضافي بما ذكرنا لأنه لا يقال للفرس «إنه جزئي إضافي للإنسان» مع إمكان فرض الاندراج.
فتأمل ليتضح لك أن الحق أن الكلي أيضاً له مفهومان :
أحدهما حقيقي يقابل مفهوم الجزئي الحقيقي تقابل العدم للملكة، وليس توقف تعقله على تعقل الغير مستلزماً لكونه إضافياً كما في الجزئي الحقيقي بعينه على ما عرفت.
وثانيهما إضافي يقابل الجزئي الإضافي تقابل التضايف، وأن الحال بين الكليين في النسبة عكس ما بين الجزئيين، فالكلّي الإضافي أخص من الحقيقي كمامر، والجزئي الإضافي أعم من الحقيقي كما سنبينه (شريف). (۱) وذلك لما عرفت أن معنى الجزئي الإضافي هو المندرج تحت غيره، وهذا هو معنى الخاص بعينه، ومعنى الكلي الإضافي هو المندرج تحته شيء آخر، وهذا هو معنى العام بعينه فالخاص والجزئي الإضافي بمعنى واحد، وكذلك العام والكلّي الإضافي بمعنى واحد، ولاشك أن الخاص والعام متضايفان مشهوران كالأب والابن وأن الخصوص والعموم متضايفان حقيقيان كالأبوة والبنوة، والمتضايفان لا يعقلان إلا معاً ، فلا يجوز أن يذكر أحدهما في تعريف الآخر، وإلا لكان تعقله قبل تعقله، ضرورة أن تعقل المعرف وأجزائه مقدم على تعقل المعرف. فإن قلت: المذكور في تعريف الجزئي الإضافي هو الأعم لا العام الذي هو بمعنى الكلى الإضافي حتى يلزم ذكر أحد المتضايفين في تعريف الآخر
If we say: 'The relative particular is that whose subsumption under something is possible,' then the relative universal would be that under which subsumption of something is possible, so it would also be more specific than the real universal, but by one degree. And it is not correct to say: 'The relative particular is that whose subsumption under something else can be assumed,' because then it would imply that the relative universal is that under which subsumption of something can be assumed, thus returning to the real meaning as before. The interpretation of the relative particular as we mentioned is not valid because one does not say of a horse 'It is a relative particular to man' despite the possibility of assuming subsumption. So reflect to clarify for you that the truth is that the universal also has two concepts: one real, opposite to the concept of the real particular by the opposition of privation and possession, and its conception's dependence on conception of another does not entail it being relative, just as in the real particular exactly as you have understood. The second is relative, opposite to the relative particular by the opposition of correlation. The situation between the two universals in terms of relation is the reverse of that between the two particulars: the relative universal is more specific than the real universal as before, and the relative particular is more general than the real particular as we will explain. That is because you have known that the meaning of the relative particular is that which is subsumed under something else, and this is exactly the meaning of the specific. And the meaning of the relative universal is that under which something else is subsumed, and this is the meaning of the general. So the specific and the relative particular are synonymous, and similarly the general and the relative universal are synonymous. There is no doubt that the specific and the general are well-known correlates like father and son, and that specificity and generality are real correlates like fatherhood and sonship. Correlates are not conceived except together, so it is not permissible to mention one of them in the definition of the other, otherwise its conception would precede its conception, necessarily because the conception of the definiendum and its parts is prior to the conception of the definiendum. If you say: What is mentioned in the definition of the relative particular is 'the more general' not 'the general' which is synonymous with the relative universal, so that would entail mentioning one correlate in the definition of the other.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage critically refines the definition of the relative particular (juz'i idafi). It begins by showing that if we define it as 'that whose subsumption under something is possible,' then the relative universal would also be defined by possibility, collapsing into the real universal. The author illustrates with an example: one does not call a horse a 'relative particular to man' just because it could be subsumed under the concept of man. The truth is that the universal also has two meanings: real and relative, opposite to the respective particulars. The relative particular is exactly the 'specific' (al-khass), and the relative universal is exactly the 'general' (al-'amm). Since specific and general are correlates like father and son, they cannot be used in each other's definitions without circularity. The passage ends by addressing an objection: perhaps the definition uses 'the more general' (a'amm) rather than 'the general' ('amm), but the argument suggests this does not avoid the problem.

Key Terms
الجزئي الإضافي
تضايف

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition of the relative particular as 'that whose subsumption under something is possible'?
A) Because it makes the relative universal also defined by possibility, collapsing into the real universal.
B) Because every particular is actually subsumed under something.
C) Because the relative particular is identical to the real particular.
D) Because subsumption cannot be assumed.

Key Takeaway
The relative particular and relative universal are correlates; defining one by the other is circular, and true definitions must respect the correlation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check whether the definition 'The relative particular is that which is more specific than something else' is circular according to this passage. Explain your reasoning.
?
In a fiqh text, the term 'khass' is defined as 'that which refers to a specific individual among a general category'. Identify whether this definition implicitly uses the correlative concept of 'amm and whether it avoids the circularity criticized here.

Critique of Defining 'Partial by Relation'
Critiques the definition of al-juz'i al-idafi and its relation to al-kulli al-idafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical defects in defining a correlative term using its correlative in Islamic logic, and evaluate alternative definitions for al-juz'i al-idafi.

Core Idea
Defining a correlative by its correlative creates a vicious circle — a foundational error in logical definition.

This passage from Qutbi's commentary critically examines the definition of 'the partial by relation' (al-juz'i al-idafi). The author shows that any definition relying on its correlative 'the universal by relation' leads to circularity. He also exposes the hidden tautology when 'the specific' is used. Understanding this critique sharpens your skill in evaluating definitions — a crucial tool in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its correlative
The term under analysis is al-juz'i al-idafi, which the author equates with al-khass (the specific). Its correlative is al-kulli al-idafi (the universal by relation). Recognize that they are correlatives (mutada'ifan) – each depends on the other for conception.
2
Analyze the first definition: 'that which can be supposed to be subsumed under something else'
This definition fails because it also applies to al-kulli al-idafi (which is 'that under which something is subsumed'), and uses the word 'kull' which refers to individuals, not suitable for a definition. Additionally, it does not distinguish between the two correlatives.
The horse example: we don't call a horse 'partial by relation to man' even though it can be subsumed, showing the definition is too broad.
3
Examine the alternative: 'the more specific than something' (al-akhass min shay')
This avoids using 'general' directly but still depends on 'al-khass' (the specific), which is synonymous with the definiendum. Thus, it leads to self-definition and indirectly involves the correlative.
The author points out that 'al-akhass' cannot be conceived without 'al-khass', causing circularity.
4
Assess the two defects and their severity
The first defect is defining the thing by itself or by something that depends on its own knowledge (stronger). The second is defining it by its correlative or by something that depends on the correlative's knowledge. Both are present, but the first is more severe. Therefore, both proposed definitions are invalid.
The author concludes that the definition by 'al-akhass min shay' includes the first defect 'necessarily'.
